Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Buying a Slave with Wine or Pig: Subsequent Dispositions Valid
Ruling on the validity of freeing, selling, or gifting a slave bought with wine or pig.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of freeing, selling, or gifting a slave bought in a corrupt sale and explain why the buyer owes the value to the original seller.

Core Idea
Possession in a corrupt sale grants ownership; subsequent dispositions are valid but the buyer must compensate the original seller.

This lesson addresses a nuanced scenario in Hanafi muamalat: a slave is purchased with wine or pig – items that cannot be valid consideration in Islamic law. After taking possession with the seller's permission, the buyer frees, sells, or gifts the slave. Is this permissible? The Hanafi school rules that such dispositions are valid because possession confers ownership even in a corrupt sale. However, the original seller's right is not extinguished; the buyer must pay the slave's market value. This ruling highlights the Hanafi emphasis on the legal effects of possession (qabd) and the distinction between binding and rescindable contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and the ruling
The buyer purchases a slave with wine or pig (a corrupt sale), takes possession with permission, then frees, sells, or gifts the slave. The ruling: these dispositions are valid (jāʼiz).
The contract itself is corrupt (fasid), but the subsequent acts are not void.
2
Locate the legal basis (dalil)
The ruling is based on the principle that possession with permission transfers ownership (al-milk bi'l-qabd). The buyer becomes owner of the slave upon possession, so his dispositions are effective.
This principle is derived from ahadith and Hanafi usul; it is considered zanni in thubut and dalala.
3
Trace the consequences for each disposition
Freeing causes the slave to 'perish' legally, so the buyer must pay the value (qīmah). Sale or gift cuts off the seller's right to reclaim; the buyer owes the value. Kitaba and rahn are similar – binding – but the right to reclaim returns if the mukatib defaults or the pledge is redeemed. Ijara is different because it is rescindable by excuse; the corruption of the sale is an excuse to rescind.
The distinction between binding (lāzim) and non-binding contracts is crucial.
4
Note the underlying principle
The Hanafi school holds that a corrupt sale with possession makes the buyer the owner, enabling valid dispositions, but the original seller retains a right to compensation because the sale was not fully valid.
Other schools may not grant ownership to this extent, but Hanafis follow the zahir al-riwayah.

If someone bought a slave in exchange for wine or pig, then took possession and freed him, or sold him, or gifted him and handed him over, then this disposition is valid and the buyer owes the value, because of what we mentioned: he owned him by possession, so his dispositions are valid; by freeing, the slave perished so the value is binding; by sale or gift, the right of reclamation is cut off as before. Kitaba (contract of manumission) and rahn (pledge) are similar to sale because both are binding, except that the right of reclamation returns upon the inability of the mukatib and upon redemption of the pledge, due to the removal of the impediment. This is contrary to ijara (lease), because it is rescinded by excuses, and removing corruption is an excuse, and because lease is concluded gradually, so return would be an obstruction. Translation: And if someone bought a slave in exchange for wine or pig, then took possession and freed him, or sold him, or gifted him and handed him over, then the disposition is valid and the buyer owes the value of this slave, due to the proof we mentioned: by possession the buyer became owner, so his dispositions are valid; by freeing, the slave perished so the value is binding; by sale or gift, the right of return is cut off as has passed. Kitaba and rahn are similar to sale because both are binding, except that the right of return returns upon the inability of the mukatib and upon redemption of the pledge, because the impediment is removed. This ruling is contrary to ijara, because it is rescinded by excuses, and removing corruption is an excuse, and because lease is concluded gradually, so return would be difficult. Explanation of the case: If someone bought a slave through a corrupt purchase, e.g., in exchange for wine or pig, then took possession with the seller's permission and freed him, or sold him, or gifted him and handed him over to the donee, then these dispositions of the buyer are valid, and the buyer owes the price of that sold slave. The buyer's dispositions are valid because when the buyer took possession with the seller's permission, the buyer became owner of the sold item.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific case in Hanafi fiqh: buying a slave with wine or pig, which is a corrupt sale because wine and pig are not valid consideration. After taking possession with the seller's permission, the buyer becomes owner of the slave, albeit through a corrupt contract. The ruling is that the buyer's subsequent dispositions (freeing, selling, gifting) are valid. However, the buyer owes the value of the slave to the original seller because his ownership was defective. The text explains that freeing causes the slave to perish (as a legal construct), so the buyer must pay the value. Selling or gifting cuts off the original seller's right to reclaim, similar to the earlier ruling. The passage also discusses kitaba (contract of manumission) and rahn (pledge): these are also binding dispositions, but if the slave falls back (mukatib unable to pay, or pledge redeemed), the original seller's right to reclaim returns. This is contrasted with ijara (lease), which is not binding in the same way because it is rescinded by excuses; here, the corruption of the original sale is considered an excuse to rescind the lease. Hence, a lease granted by the buyer would be invalid and the original seller can reclaim the slave. The lesson consolidates the general principle that possession in a corrupt sale confers ownership for the purpose of valid dispositions, but the original seller retains a right to compensation.

Key Terms
قیمت (qīmah): The monetary value (price) of the slave, owed by the buyer to the original seller because the slave is now gone (freed or sold).
کتابت (kitābah): A contract of manumission where a slave agrees to pay his master a sum in installments; upon payment, he becomes free.
اجارہ (ijārah): A lease contract; here distinguished from sale because it can be rescinded due to excuses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone buys a slave in exchange for wine (a corrupt sale), takes possession with permission, and then frees the slave, what is the legal effect?
A) The freeing is invalid and the slave remains in the ownership of the original seller.
B) The freeing is valid and the buyer owes the value of the slave to the original seller.
C) The freeing is valid and the buyer owes nothing because the sale was corrupt.
D) The freeing is invalid, and the buyer must return the slave to the original seller.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, possession in a corrupt sale confers ownership for valid dispositions, but the buyer must compensate the original seller with the item's value.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
12 min
Validity of Disposals after Fasid Sale of a Slave
Ruling with proof via talil and analogies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that possession in a fasid sale confers ownership, and identify which disposals cut off the seller's right of rescission (istirdad) and which do not.

Core Idea
Possession in a fasid sale makes the buyer the owner — disposals are valid but value is owed.

In Hanafi fiqh, a fasid sale (e.g., buying a slave with wine) is not completely void. If the buyer takes possession with the seller's permission, they become the owner. This transforms the legal landscape: the buyer's subsequent actions—freeing, selling, gifting, or even making a manumission contract or pledge—are valid and can block the original seller's right to cancel the sale and reclaim the slave. However, the buyer must pay the slave's market value. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind these rulings and the crucial distinction between different types of disposal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its conditions
The passage rules that if a slave is bought in a fasid sale (price: wine or pork), taken possession with permission, and then freed, sold, or gifted, the disposal is valid. The buyer must pay the slave's value (qimah). Note the key condition: possession with seller's permission.
2
Understand the proof: possession confers ownership
The reason (talil) is that possession in a fasid sale makes the buyer the owner (milkiyyah). This is a Hanafi principle. Once owner, all disposals are effective. Freeing destroys the subject, so value is owed analogously to a usurper. Selling/gifting creates third-party rights that cut off the seller's rescission right.
This differs from other schools who do not recognize ownership in fasid sales.
3
Trace the analogy for kitabah and rahn
Kitabah (contract of manumission) and rahn (pledge) are like sale because they are binding contracts. They also cut off rescission, but unlike sale, the right can return if the mukatab fails or the pledge is redeemed.
4
Distinguish ijarah (lease) from the others
Ijarah does NOT cut off rescission. Why? Because ijarah can be rescinded due to legal excuses (like removing a defect in the sale), and it is contracted gradually (usufruct is generated over time). The seller can rescind the fasid sale and terminate the lease.
This is a key exception showing the nuanced application of the principle.

Source Text

واپس لینے کا اختیار ختم ہو گیا ہے اور ہبہ کی صورت میں موہوب لہ کا حق متعلق ہو گیا ہے اور سابق میں گذر چکا کہ حق عبد حق شرع پر مقدم ہوتا ہے یعنی حق عبد یعنی مشتری ثانی اور موہوب لہ کے حق کے متعلق ہونے کی وجہ سے بیچ فاسد کو مسخ کر کے مبیع بائع اول کو واپس نہیں کی جائے گی ۔

اور کتابت اور رہن رکھنا بیچ کی نظیر ہے یعنی اگر بیع فاسد کے طور پر غلام خرید کر قبضہ کر کے مشتری نے اس کو مکاتب کرد یا یا غلام کوکسی کے پاس رہن رکھدیا تو جس طرح مشتری کے غلام کو فروخت کرنے کی صورت میں بائع اول کا حق استرداد ساقط ہو جاتا ہے اسی طرح کتابت اور رہن کی صورت میں بائع اول کا حق استرداد ساقط ہوتا ہے۔ اس طرح کتابت اور رہن کی صورت میں بائع اول کا حق استرداد ساقط ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ کتابت اور رہن دونوں بیچ کی طرح لازم ہیں۔ چنانچہ مرتبہن کے شئی مرہونہ پر قبضہ کرنے کے بعد رہن را ہن کے حق میں لازم ہو جاتا ہے اور کتابت مولی کے حق میں لازم ہو جاتی ہے اور کتابت کی صورت میں خود مکاتب کا حق اپنی ذات کے ساتھ متعلق ہو جاتا ہے اور رہن کی صورت میں مرتین کا حق متعلق ہو جاتا ہے پس مرتبہن اور مکاتب کے حق کے متعلق ہونے کی وجہ سے بیع فاسد مسخ کر کے مبیع ، بائع اول کی طرف واپس نہیں کی جائے گی ہاں اگر مکاتب بدل کتابت ادا کرنے سے عاجز آ گیا یا را بہن نے قرضہ ادا کر کے شنی مرہونہ کو چھڑالیا تو بائع کا حق استرداد عود کر آئے گا کیونکہ حق استرداد کے عود کرنے سے جو چیز مانع تھی یعنی مبیع کے ساتھ حق عبد کا متعلق ہونا وہ زائل ہوگئی ہے پس جب مانع زائل ہو گیا تو بائع کا بیع فاسد ضح کر کے مبیع واپس لینے کا حق بھی عود کر آئے گا۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ آزادی، بیج، بہ مقبوضہ کی وجہ سے بائع کے حق استرداد کا ساقط ہو جانا اجارہ کے برخلاف ہے یعنی اگر کسی نے بیچ فاسد کے طور پر کوئی چیز خرید کر اس پر قبضہ کرنے کے بعد اس چیز کو اجارہ پر دے دیا تو اس اجارہ کی وجہ سے بالکع کا حق استرداد ساقط نہ ہوگا بلکہ بائع کو بیع فاسد فسخ کر کے بیچ واپس لینے کا اختیار باقی رہے گا کیونکہ اجارہ انذار کی وجہت مسیح کیا جا سکتا ہے اور فساد کو اور کرنا قومی عذر ہے پس اس عذر کی وجہ سے اجارہ نسخ ہو جائے گا اور جب اجارہ فتح ہو گیا تو بائع بیع فاسد کو فتح کر کے مبیع واپس لے سکتا ہے۔ دوسری دلیل یہ ہے کہ اجارہ کسی چیز کے منافع حاصل کرنے پر منعقد ہوتا ہے اور تمام منافع بالفعل موجود نہیں ہوتے بلکہ تھورے تھوڑے وقتا فوقتا پیدا ہوتے ہیں تو انھیں کے موافق اجارہ بھی تھوڑا تھوڑا کر کے منعقد ہوتا ہے پس جس وقت اجارہ کو ختم کرنا چاہے گا تو یہ
If someone buys a slave in exchange for wine or pork, then takes possession and frees him, or sells him, or gives him as a gift and hands him over, then this disposal is valid, and he owes the value (price). The proof: by taking possession he became owner, so his disposals are effective. By freeing, the slave is destroyed, so value is owed. By sale or gift, the right of rescission is cut off as previously mentioned. Kitabah (contract of manumission) and rahn (pledge) are analogous to sale because they are binding. However, the right of rescission returns upon the inability of the mukatab or the redemption of the pledge due to removal of the impediment. This is contrary to ijarah (lease) because ijarah is rescinded due to excuses and removing the defect is an excuse, and because ijarah is contracted gradually, so returning it constitutes prevention. Explanation of the case: If someone buys a slave via a fasid sale, e.g., in exchange for wine or pork, then takes possession with the seller's permission and frees him, or sells him, or gives him as a gift and hands him over to the donee, these disposals are valid. The buyer owes the value of the slave. The disposals are valid because when the buyer took possession with the seller's permission, he became owner [صبیح?] because according to us (Hanafis), a fasid sale with possession confers ownership. Thus when the buyer became owner, all his disposals over the slave are effective. The buyer owes the value because in a fasid sale, the sold item is guaranteed by itself (mudman bi nafsihi), meaning if it is from things with equivalents, the equivalent is due; if from things of value, the value is due. Here the slave is from things of value, so the value is due. The author of Hidayah says: when the buyer took possession and freed the slave, the slave's value no longer remains, so it is as if the slave is destroyed, and the value is due, just as a usurped slave if destroyed under the usurper, the usurper owes value. If the buyer sold or gifted and handed over, in both cases the original seller's right to rescind the sale and take back the slave is cut off, because in the case of gift the donee's right attaches. It has passed previously that the right of a servant (third party) takes precedence over purely legal rights. Thus due to the attachment of the second buyer's or donee's right, the fasid sale cannot be rescinded to return it to the original seller. Kitabah and rahn are analogous to sale: if after purchasing in a fasid sale and taking possession, the buyer makes the slave a mukatab or pledges him, the original seller's right of rescission drops just as in the case of sale. This is because kitabah and rahn are both binding. When the pledgee takes possession, the pledge becomes binding for the pledger, and kitabah becomes binding for the master. In kitabah, the mukatab's right attaches to his own person; in rahn, the pledgee's right attaches. Thus due to the attachment of the pledgee's or mukatab's right, the fasid sale cannot be rescinded to return the slave to the original seller. However, if the mukatab becomes unable to pay or the pledger redeems the pledge by paying the debt, the right of rescission returns because the impediment (the attached right) is removed. When the impediment is gone, the seller's right to rescind the fasid sale and take back the slave returns. The author says: the dropping of the seller's right of rescission due to freedom, sale, or possession is contrary to ijarah. That is, if someone buys something in a fasid sale, takes possession, then leases it out, the seller's right of rescission does not drop; rather, the seller retains the option to rescind the fasid sale and take back the sold item, because ijarah can be rescinded due to excuses and removing the defect is an excuse. Thus due to this excuse, the ijarah is rescinded, and once the ijarah is rescinded, the seller can rescind the fasid sale and take back the item. A second proof: ijarah is contracted for the benefit of usufruct, and all usufructs are not present at once but are generated gradually over time; accordingly, ijarah is also contracted gradually. So when one wishes to terminate it, that [termination] constitutes prevention.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (talil) from the Hidayah's commentary on the section of fasid sales. The core ruling: if a slave is purchased in a fasid sale (e.g., with wine or pork) and the buyer takes possession with the seller's permission, then any subsequent disposal (freeing, selling, gifting, making a mukatab, or pledging) is valid, and the buyer must pay the slave's market value (qimah). The reasoning is that possession in a fasid sale, when permitted by the seller, confers ownership (milkiyyah) in Hanafi law. Therefore, the buyer's actions as owner are effective. Freeing the slave extinguishes the subject matter, so the buyer owes its value by analogy to a usurper. Selling or gifting cuts off the seller's right of rescission (istirdad) because a third party's right (the buyer's buyer or donee) attaches, and such rights (huquq al-'ibad) take precedence over the seller's contractual right. Kitabah (manumission by contract) and rahn (pledging) are treated like sale in that they are binding contracts; once concluded, the seller cannot rescind. However, if the mukatab fails to pay or the pledger redeems the pledge, the seller's right returns because the impediment (the third-party right) has been removed. In contrast, ijarah (lease) does not cut off the right of rescission, because ijarah can be rescinded due to a valid excuse (like removing a defect), and it is contracted gradually over time, so the seller can rescind it to reclaim the item. This shows the Hanafi method of talil: the ruling is derived from the underlying effective cause (illa) of ownership by possession and the principle of third-party rights.

Key Terms
بیع فاسد (bay' fasid): A sale that is defective in its consideration (e.g., exchange for wine or pork) but otherwise meets the pillars of a contract.
قبض (qabd): Taking physical possession of the sold item with the seller's permission.
استرداد (istirdad): The original seller's right to rescind the fasid sale and reclaim the sold item.
ذوات القیم (dhawat al-qiyam): Items that do not have exact equivalents in the market and must be compensated by market value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a fasid sale of a slave (price: wine), the buyer takes possession with permission and then sells the slave to a third party. What is the effect on the original seller's right of rescission?
A) The seller can rescind the fasid sale and reclaim the slave from the third party.
B) The seller's right of rescission is cut off because the sale to the third party creates a binding right.
C) The seller can rescind only if the third party agrees.
D) The seller's right remains but he can only claim the value from the buyer.

Key Takeaway
Possession in a fasid sale confers ownership, making the buyer's subsequent disposals valid, but the buyer must pay the market value; the seller's right of rescission is cut off by third-party rights except in cases like ijarah where it can be terminated.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
12 min
Effects of Buyer's Actions on Seller's Right to Rescind a Fasid Sale
Explains when seller's rescission right is lost

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which acts of the buyer in a fasid sale extinguish the seller's right of rescission and explain why ijarah is an exception.

Core Idea
Ijarah is the only disposition that does not extinguish the seller's right to rescind a fasid sale.

In a defective sale (bay' fasid), the buyer becomes owner upon possession with permission. However, the seller retains a right to rescind and reclaim the object. This lesson examines which subsequent actions by the buyer cause that right to be lost—and why ijarah stands apart.

Guided Path
1
Understand the nature of a fasid sale
Identify that a fasid sale transfers ownership once the buyer takes possession with the seller's permission. The buyer becomes liable for the price: value (qimah) for dhawat al-qimah (e.g., a slave) or equivalent (mithl) for mithliyat.
This is a distinct Hanafi position; other schools may consider such a sale void.
2
Identify acts that extinguish the seller's right
List the buyer's actions that create a third-party right: selling to another, gifting with delivery, making a kitabah contract (mukatib), or pledging as rahn. The principle is that the third party's right (haqq al-'abd) takes precedence over the seller's legal right (haqq shar'i).
These actions are binding (lazim) and cannot be undone by the seller's rescission.
3
Analyze the case of freeing the slave
When the buyer frees the slave, the mabii' is effectively destroyed (the value ceases). The buyer must pay its value by analogy (qiyas) to ghasp (usurpation): the usurper pays value for a destroyed item.
Freeing is like a disposition that removes the mabii' from existence, so no third-party right attaches, but the right to rescind is lost because the object no longer exists.
4
Contrast ijarah (lease)
Ijarah does not extinguish the right because it can be annulled due to a legal excuse (like the invalidity of the underlying sale). Moreover, ijarah is incremental: benefits arise over time, so the seller can terminate the lease for the future. Hence, the seller can still rescind the fasid sale and reclaim the mabii'.
Two proofs: (1) ijarah is non-binding if an excuse arises; (2) benefits are not all present at once, so the lease can be stopped.
5
Note the return of the right
If the kitabah fails (mukatib unable to pay) or the rahn is redeemed (pledgor pays debt), the obstacle is removed and the seller's right to rescind returns.
This shows the right is not permanently lost but suspended while the third-party right exists.

پر دینے سے رکنے کا اختیار اس کو ہمہ وقت ہے اس لئے مذکورہ صورت میں اجارہ تو ڑ کر مبیع بائع کی طرف واپس کرنا واجب ہے۔
Book of Sales. Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu commentary volume eight: The buyer became owner because in our view, in a defective sale where possession is taken with the seller's permission, the sale confers ownership. Thus when the buyer becomes owner of the sold item, all his dispositions within it are effective. The buyer owes the price because in a defective sale, the sale is a guarantee in itself, meaning if the sold item is of the genus of things with equivalents, the equivalent is due; if of things of value, the value is due. In this issue, the slave is of the things of value, so the buyer owes the slave's value. The author of al-Hidayah says: In a defective sale, when the buyer took possession and freed the slave, the slave's market value ceased; thus the slave is like destroyed, and the value of a destroyed slave is due, so the buyer owes the slave's value, as in the case of a usurped slave destroyed by the usurper. If the buyer sold the slave or gifted it and delivered it, in both cases the first seller's right to rescind the sale and reclaim the sold item is extinguished. In the case of gift, the donee's right attaches, and it has passed that the right of a slave is preferred over a legal right, i.e., due to the attachment of the second buyer's or donee's right, the defective sale will not be rescinded to return the sold item to the first seller. Kitabah and rahn are analogous to sale: if the buyer bought a slave in a defective sale, took possession, then made a kitabah contract or pledged the slave, just as in the case of the buyer's selling, the first seller's right of rescission is dropped, similarly in kitabah and rahn. Because both kitabah and rahn are binding like sale. After the pledgee takes possession of the pledged item, the rahn becomes binding on the pledgor, and kitabah becomes binding on the master. In kitabah, the mukatib's right attaches to his person; in rahn, the pledgee's right attaches. Thus due to the attachment of the pledgee's and mukatib's rights, the defective sale will not be rescinded to return the sold item to the first seller. However, if the mukatib becomes unable to pay or the pledgor pays the debt and redeems the pledged item, the seller's right of rescission returns because the obstacle (the attachment of another's right) has been removed. The author of al-Hidayah says: The dropping of the seller's right of rescission due to freeing, selling, and possession is contrary to ijarah. That is, if someone bought an item in a defective sale, took possession, then leased it, his right of rescission is not dropped; rather, the seller retains the option to rescind the defective sale and reclaim the sold item because ijarah can be annulled due to a legal excuse, and annulling the sale is a valid excuse. So due to this excuse, the lease is annulled, and when the lease is annulled, the seller can annul the defective sale and take back the sold item. The second proof is that ijarah is contracted for the benefit, and not all benefits exist at once; they arise little by little over time. Accordingly, ijarah is also contracted incrementally. So whenever he wants to terminate the lease, he stops contracting for the future benefit. He always has the option to stop leasing, so in this case, it is obligatory to break the lease and return the sold item to the seller.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on al-Hidayah explaining the effects of a buyer's actions in a fasid (defective) sale on the seller's right of rescission (haqq al-istirdad). The author first establishes that in a fasid sale, once the buyer takes possession with the seller's permission, ownership transfers to the buyer. Consequently, the buyer is liable for the price — specifically the value (qimah) if the mabii' is a thing of value (dhawat al-qimah), as in the case of a slave. The commentary then addresses several scenarios. If the buyer frees the slave, the value is still due by analogy to ghasp (usurpation). If the buyer sells or gifts the mabii' to another, the original seller's right to rescind is lost because a third party's right (the second buyer or donee) attaches, and according to Hanafi principle, the right of a person (haqq al-'abd) takes precedence over a legal right (haqq shar'i). Similarly, if the buyer enters a kitabah contract or pledges the mabii' as rahn, these are binding contracts (lazim), so the seller cannot rescind. However, if the mukatib fails to pay or the pledgor redeems, the obstacle is removed and the seller's right returns. Ijarah (lease) is distinguished: it does not prevent rescission because it can be annulled due to a legal excuse (like the invalidity of the underlying sale), and because benefits arise incrementally, the lease can be terminated at any point for the future. Thus, the seller can rescind the fasid sale and reclaim the mabii' even if it is leased.

Key Terms
بیع فاسد (bay' fasid): A sale that is defective due to a missing condition (e.g., unknown price) but not fundamentally invalid; it transfers ownership upon possession with permission.
مبیع (mabii'): The object of sale, i.e., the slave in this context.
حق استرداد (haqq al-istirdad): The seller's right to rescind the defective sale and reclaim the mabii'.
ذوات القیم (dhawat al-qimah): Items that do not have a standard equivalent in the market, such as a slave; their value is assessed individually.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a defective sale (bay' fasid) of a slave, if the buyer leases the slave to a third party before the seller rescinds, does the seller still have the right to rescind?
A) Yes, because ijarah can be annulled due to a legal excuse.
B) No, because ijarah is a binding contract like rahn and kitabah.
C) Yes, but only if the lease contract explicitly allows cancellation.
D) No, because the buyer's right of ownership overrides the seller's right.

Key Takeaway
The seller's right to rescind a fasid sale is lost when the buyer disposes of the object in a manner that attaches an irrevocable third-party right, but ijarah does not qualify because it can be annulled.

Seller Must Return Price Before Reclaiming Mabii' in Fasid Sale
Ruling with proof: seller's condition for reclaiming mabii' in fasid sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for the seller to reclaim the mabii' in a fasid sale and explain the qiyas to rahn and ghasp.

Core Idea
The seller cannot retake the sold item without first returning the price—mabii' is held as collateral.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi ruling on defective sales (buyu' fasidah). When a sale is fasid, the seller has the right to rescind, but that right is conditional on returning whatever he received from the buyer. The text from al-Hidayah uses analogies to a pledge (rahn) and usurpation (ghasp) to justify this principle and also clarifies the status of the price.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State the seller's obligation before reclaiming the mabii' in a fasid sale. The text says: 'The seller does not have the right to take the sold item until he returns the price.'
The 'price' here may refer to the value (qimah) owed by the buyer.
2
Understand the rahn analogy
The mabii' is held by the buyer as collateral for the price, like a pledged item secures a debt. This gives the buyer a right to retain possession until the price is returned.
If the seller dies, the buyer's priority over the mabii' continues over heirs and creditors, mirroring the pledgee's priority.
3
Analyze the ghasp analogy
The fasid sale is likened to ghasp to determine whether the price is returned in specie or by value. The correct view (asahh) is that specific dirhams are determined; if they exist, they are returned exactly; if consumed, their equivalent.
This resolves the dispute: some held that the price is not determined and only value is owed.
4
Note the practical consequence
The seller must return what he actually received from the buyer, whether goods or currency, before reclaiming the mabii'. This prevents unjust enrichment.
Ibn al-Humam adds that the value is not strictly due; the seller returns whatever he took.

Source Text

قال وليس للبائع فى البيع الفاسد ان ياخذ المبيع حتى يرد الثمن لأن المبيع مقابل به قیصیر محبوسا به کالرهن وان مات البائع والمشترى احق به حتى يستوفى الثمن لانه يقدم عليه في حياته فكذا على روتته وغرمائه کیونکہ دراہم بیج فاسد میں متعین ہو جاتے ہیں اور یہی قول اصح ہے کیونکہ بیچ فاسد بمنزلہ غصب کے ہے اور اگر شمن کے درہم تلف کر دیئے گئے تو ان کا مثل لے لے اس دلیل کی وجہ سے جو ہم نے بیان کی ہے۔ تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ بیع فاسد کی صورت میں بائع اگر مشتری سے مبیع واپس لینا چاہے تو بائع پہلے مشتری کاشن واپس کرے صاحب عنایہ نے نہایہ کے حوالہ سے تحریر کیا ہے کہ یہاں شمن سے مراد قیمت ہے کیونکہ بیع فاسد میں مشتری پر مبیع کی قیمت واجب ہوتی ہے ثمن واجب نہیں ہوتا پس جب بائع نے مشتری سے قیمت لی ہے تو قیمت ہی کو واپس کرنا ضروری ہوگا علامہ ابن الہمام نے فتح القدیر میں لکھا ہے کہ قیمت بھی لازم نہیں ہے کیونکہ بیچ فاسد میں مشتری پر بلاشبہ قیمت واجب ہوتی ہے لیکن اگر بائع اور مشتری شمن پر اتفاق کر لیں تو بائع ثمن لے لیتا ہے پس مصنف کو یوں کہنا چاہئے تھا کہ بیع فاسد میں بائع کو بیچ واپس لینے کا حق نہیں ہے یہاں تک کہ بائع وہ چیز واپس کر دے جو اس نے مبیع کے عوض مشتری سے لی ہے خواہ وہ چیز از قبیلہ سامان ہو یا از قبیلۂ نقود ہو اور نقود دشمن کے طور پر ہوں یا قیمت کے طور پر حاصل یہ کہ پہلے بائع وہ چیز مشتری کو واپس کرے جو اس نے مشتری سے مبیع کے عوض لی ہے پھر مبیع واپس لے۔ کیونکہ مبیع اسی چیز کے مقابل ہے جو مشتری نے بائع کو دی ہے پس اس کے عوض مبیع مشتری کے پاس محبوس رہے گی یعنی جب تک بائع مبیع کا عوض واپس نہیں کرے گا تب تک بائع کو بیچ واپس لینے کا حق حاصل نہ ہو گا جیسے شئی مرہونہ قرضہ کے عوض محبوس رہتی ہے یعنی جب تک راہن قرضہ ادا نہیں کرے گا تب تک مرتین کوشئی مرہونہ کے روکنے کا حق حاصل رہے گا اور اگر بائع کی وفات ہوگئی تو مشتری مبیع کا زیادہ حقدار ہے یہاں تک کہ مشتری اپنا دیا ہوا پورا پوراشن وصول کرلے کیونکہ جب مشتری بائع کی زندگی میں مبیع کا زیادہ حقدار تھا تو اس کے مرنے کے بعد بھی اس کے وارثوں اور قرض خواہوں کے مقابلہ میں زیادہ حقدار ہو گا جیسا کہ اگر راہن مر گیا اور راہن کے ورثاء اور قرض خواہ زندہ ہیں تو مال مرہونہ کا زیادہ حقدار مرتہن ہو گا یعنی پہلے مرتہن شئی مرہونہ سے اپنا قرضہ وصول کرے گا اس کے بعد اگر کچھ باقی رہا تو وہ قرض خواہوں اور ورثاء کو دیا جائے گا صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ مشتری نے بیچ کے عوض جو درا ہم یا دنانیر بائع کو دیئے تھے اگر وہ بائع
Can the seller take back the sold item in a defective sale or not? He said: The seller in a defective sale does not have the right to take the sold item until he returns the price, because the sold item is in exchange for it and is thus held as collateral like a pledge. If the seller dies, the buyer has greater right to it until he receives his price, because he had priority over the seller during his life, similarly over his heirs and creditors after his death, like a pledgor. Then if the dirhams of the price are still in existence, he takes them exactly because they are determined in a defective sale, and this is the correct view because it is in the category of usurpation. If they are consumed, he takes their equivalent for the reason we stated. Translation: In a defective sale, the seller does not have the right to take back the sold item until he returns the price, because the sold item is in exchange for it, so it remains held as collateral like a pledge. If the seller dies, the buyer has greater right to this sold item until he receives his full price, because he was given priority over the seller during his life, so similarly after his death over his heirs and creditors, like a pledgor (if he dies). Then if the dirhams of the price are still in existence, he takes them exactly because the dirhams are determined in a defective sale, and this is the correct view because the defective sale is like usurpation. If the dirhams are consumed, he takes their equivalent for the reason we stated. Explanation: The scenario is that in a defective sale, if the seller wishes to take back the sold item from the buyer, the seller must first return the buyer's price. The author of al-'Inayah wrote with reference to al-Nihayah that here 'price' means the value, because in a defective sale the buyer owes the value of the sold item, not the price. So when the seller has taken the value from the buyer, it is necessary to return that value. Ibn al-Humam wrote in Fath al-Qadir that the value is not necessary either, because in a defective sale the buyer undoubtedly owes the value, but if the seller and buyer agree on a price, the seller takes the price. So the author should have said: In a defective sale, the seller does not have the right to take back the sold item until he returns whatever he took from the buyer in exchange for the sold item, whether it is goods or currency, and whether the currency is as a price or as value. The gist is that first the seller returns to the buyer whatever he took from him in exchange for the sold item, then takes back the sold item. Because the sold item is in exchange for that thing the buyer gave the seller. Thus the sold item remains held in possession of the buyer as collateral, meaning as long as the seller does not return the equivalent of the sold item, the seller does not have the right to take back the sold item, just as the pledged item remains held in exchange for the debt, i.e., as long as the pledgor does not pay the debt, the pledgee has the right to detain the pledged item. If the seller dies, the buyer has greater right to the sold item until he receives his full payment, because when the buyer had greater right to the sold item during the seller's life, similarly after his death in comparison to his heirs and creditors, just as if the pledgor dies and the pledgor's heirs and creditors are alive, the pledgee has greater right to the pledged property: first the pledgee recovers his debt from the pledged property, then if something remains, it is given to the creditors and heirs. The author of al-Hidayah says: The dirhams or dinars that the buyer gave to the seller in exchange for the sold item, if the seller...
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage begins with a heading asking whether the seller can reclaim the mabii' in a fasid sale, followed by a direct quote from al-Hidayah and its Urdu commentary. The ruling is clear: the seller cannot take back the sold item until he returns the price (thaman) or value (qimah) he received. The reason is that the mabii' is held as collateral (mahbus) in exchange for the price, analogous to a rahn (pledge) where the pledged item secures the debt. If the seller dies, the buyer has priority over the mabii' over the seller's heirs and creditors, just as a pledgee has priority over the pledged item. This is because the buyer's right was already established during the seller's life. The passage also discusses whether the price is determined in a fasid sale. The correct view (asahh) is that if the exact dirhams of the price remain, the seller takes them back exactly; if they are consumed, he takes their equivalent (mithl). This is because a fasid sale is akin to ghasp (usurpation), where the usurper returns the exact property if it exists, otherwise its value. The commentary further clarifies that 'price' here may refer to the value (qimah) since in a fasid sale the buyer owes the value, not the original price. However, if the parties agree on a price, that can be accepted. A nuanced point from Ibn al-Humam is that the value is also not strictly due; rather, the seller must return whatever he actually took from the buyer, whether goods or currency. The key principle is that the seller cannot exercise his right to rescind without first returning what he received from the buyer.

Key Terms
ثمن (thaman): The price paid by the buyer in the fasid sale; in this context it may refer to the value (qimah) owed by the buyer.
محبوس (mahbus): An item held as security or collateral; here the mabii' is held by the buyer until the seller returns the price.
غصب (ghasp): Usurpation; the text draws an analogy between a fasid sale and ghasp regarding the return of the subject matter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a defective sale (bay' fasid), what must the seller do before he can take back the sold item (mabii') from the buyer?
A) He must rescind the contract verbally.
B) He must return the price (thaman) or value he received from the buyer.
C) He must obtain the buyer's permission.
D) He must wait until the buyer sells the item.

Key Takeaway
In a fasid sale, the seller's right to reclaim the sold item is conditional on returning the price he received, by analogy to a pledge.

Seller Must Return Price Before Reclaiming Sold Item in Fasid Sale
Ruling on seller's obligation to return price before reclaiming sold item in fasid sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the seller's right to reclaim the sold item in a fasid sale and explain the qiyas to rahn and ghasp that justifies it.

Core Idea
In a fasid sale, the seller cannot reclaim the sold item until returning the price — the item is detained like a pledge.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a practical scenario: when a sale is fasid (invalid), the seller may want the item back, but can he simply take it? The ruling is that the seller must first return whatever price he received. The author provides proofs based on analogy to pledge (rahn) and usurpation (ghasp), illustrating Hanafi methodology in deriving transactional rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key ruling
Read the first sentence: 'ليس للبائع في البيع الفاسد أن يأخذ المبيع حتى يرد الثمن'. What is the seller prohibited from doing?
The prohibition is conditional: until the price is returned.
2
Understand the basis
Why is the sold item compared to a pledge (rahn)? The text says 'لأن المبيع مقابل به محبوس به كالرهن'. Identify the common element (illa) in this analogy.
Both are held in exchange for a right; the illa is 'holding in exchange for a right'.
3
Trace the post-death scenario
If the seller dies, the buyer has more right to the sold item than the seller's heirs and creditors until the price is paid. This parallels the pledgee's priority over the pledgor's estate.
The analogy to rahn continues in inheritance and creditor priority.
4
Distinguish the price treatment
The text distinguishes two cases: if the price dirhams are still in existence, the buyer takes them exactly; if consumed, the buyer takes their equivalent (mithl). Why? Because in a fasid sale the price becomes specified (ta'ayyun), unlike a valid sale.
This specification is justified by analogy to ghasp (usurpation) — the seller's possession is akin to wrongful possession.
5
Recognize the analogical reasoning
The passage uses qiyas on two levels: (1) detention of the sold item is like rahn; (2) specification of the price is like ghasp. Work through the structure: asl (original case), hukm asl, illah, far' (new case), hukm far'.
This shows how Hanafi fiqh extends rulings from established cases to new situations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وليس للبائع فى البيع الفاسد ان ياخذ المبيع حتى يرد الثمن لأن المبيع مقابل به قیصیر محبوسا به كالرهن وان مات البائع والمشترى احق به حتى يستوفى الثمن لانه يقدم عليه في حياته فكذا على ورثته وغرمائه بعد وفاته كالراهن ثم ان كانت دراهم الثمن قائمة ياخذها بعينها لانها تتعين في البيع الفاسد وهو الاصح لا نه بمنزلة الغصب وان كانت مستهلكة اخذ مثلها لما بينا
He said: The seller in an invalid sale does not have the right to take back the sold item until he returns the price, because the sold item is held in exchange for it, detained like a pledge. If the seller dies, the buyer has more right to it until he receives the full price, because he was given priority over the seller in his lifetime, so likewise over his heirs and creditors after his death, like the pledgor. Then if the dirhams of the price are still in existence, he takes them precisely because they become specified in the invalid sale, and this is the most sound opinion, because it is analogous to usurpation. And if they were consumed, he takes their equivalent, as we have explained.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage states a ruling regarding fasid (invalid) sales. The seller cannot unilaterally reclaim the sold item unless he first returns whatever he received as the price. The sold item is considered detained in exchange for the price, similar to a pledge (rahn). If the seller dies, the buyer retains priority over the sold item over the seller's heirs and creditors until he recovers the full price, analogous to the pledgee's right. Regarding the price: if the specific coins or goods paid are still in the seller's possession, the buyer takes them back exactly; if they were consumed, the buyer takes their equivalent (mithl). This is because in a fasid sale, the price becomes specified (ta'ayyun) unlike in valid sales, and the seller's possession over the price is akin to usurpation (ghasp). The ruling is based on qiyas to rahn and ghasp.

Key Terms
البيع الفاسد (al-bay‘ al-fāsid): A sale that is invalid in essence due to a deficiency in a pillar or condition, but may be rectified or differ from batil.
ثمن (thaman): Price or payment given in exchange for the sold item. In fasid sale, it becomes specified.
رهن (rahn): Pledge; an item held as security for a debt. Used as analogical basis for detention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a fasid (invalid) sale, when can the seller reclaim the sold item from the buyer?
A) At any time without any condition.
B) Only after the seller returns the price (or its value) to the buyer.
C) Only if the buyer agrees.
D) Never, because the sale is void.

Key Takeaway
In a fasid sale, the seller must return the price before reclaiming the sold item, and if the price is consumed, its equivalent must be returned.

Buyer's Construction on Fasid Property: Pay Value or Demolish?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on buyer's construction on fasid property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding buyer's construction on fasid property, explain their proofs, and identify the practical consequence of the dispute.

Core Idea
Seller's implicit authorization (taslit) can nullify his right to reclaim land from a fasid buyer, but the Sahibayn disagree.

In a fasid (voidable) sale, the buyer may have taken possession and even built on the land. Does that building cut off the seller's right to reclaim? The Hanafi school is divided: Abu Hanifa says the buyer owes the land's value and keeps the building; his two disciples insist the building be demolished. Let us examine their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State clearly: Abu Hanifa: buyer pays value (qimah) and keeps building. Sahibayn: building must be demolished and land returned. Note the planting is treated similarly.
Ya'qub doubted the narration from Abu Hanifa, but Muhammad confirmed the dispute.
2
Understand the Sahibayn's analogy
The Sahibayn compare the seller's right to the pre-emptor's right (shuf'a). The pre-emptor's right is weaker yet not nullified by building; the seller's right is stronger, so even more protected. Therefore, the building should be removed.
Pre-emptor's right requires court action and lapses by delay.
3
Understand Abu Hanifa's response
Abu Hanifa distinguishes via authorization (taslit). The seller implicitly allowed the buyer to use the land by delivering possession. Building is permanent and with permission, so the seller's right to reclaim is cut off, analogous to a valid sale.
No such authorization exists from a pre-emptor, so the analogy fails.
4
Note the practical consequence
The dispute leads to different outcomes: under Abu Hanifa, buyer keeps building but pays land value; under Sahibayn, buyer demolishes at own cost and returns the land. This affects financial liability and property use.
Later Hanafi fatwa practice may prefer Abu Hanifa for practicality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال و من باع دارا بيعا فاسدا فبناها المشترى فعليه قيمتها عند ابي حنيفة رواه يعقوب عنه في الجامع الصغير ثم شک بعد ذالك فى الرواية وقالا ينقض البناء وترد الدار والغرس على هذا الاختلاف لهما ان حق الشفيع اضعف من حق البائع حتى يحتاج فيه الى القضاء ويبطل بالتاخير بخلاف حق البائع ثم اضعف الحقين لا يبطل بالبناء فاقواهما اولى وله ان البنا والغرس مما يقصد به الدوام وقد حصل بتسليط من جهة البائع فينقطع حق الاسترداد كالبيع بخلاف حق الشفيع لانه لم يوجد منه التسليط ولهذا لا يبطل بهبة المشترى وبيعه فكذابنائه وشك يعقوب في حفظه الرواية عن ابي حنيفة وقد نص محمد على الاختلاف في كتاب الشفعة فان حق الشفعة مبنى على انقطاع حق البائع بالبناء وثبوته على الاختلاف
He said: If someone sells a house in a fasid sale and the buyer builds on it, then according to Abu Hanifa the buyer owes its value. Ya‘qub narrated this from him in al-Jami‘ al-Saghir, then later doubted the narration. But the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: The building is demolished and the house is returned; similarly for planting. Their evidence is that the right of the pre-emptor (shafi‘) is weaker than the seller's right – it requires a court order and is lost by delay, unlike the seller's right. The weaker of the two rights is not nullified by building, so the stronger is more entitled to remain. His (Abu Hanifa's) evidence is that the building and planting are intended to be permanent, and they occurred with the seller's authorization, so the right of return is cut off, as in a valid sale, unlike the pre-emptor's right, because no authorization from him existed; therefore, the pre-emptor's right is not nullified by the buyer's gift or sale, nor by his building. Ya‘qub doubted his preservation of the narration from Abu Hanifa, but Muhammad explicitly stated the disagreement in Kitab al-Shuf‘a. Indeed, the right of pre-emption is based on whether the seller's right is cut off by building or not, and its establishment is disputed.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a buyer who builds on land purchased through a fasid (invalid) sale. Abu Hanifa holds that the seller's right to reclaim the land is cut off by the building, so the buyer must pay the value of the land (without demolishing the building). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the building must be demolished and the land returned to the seller. The Sahibayn argue by comparison to the right of pre-emption (shuf‘a): the pre-emptor's right is weaker than the seller's right, yet even that weaker right is not nullified by the buyer's construction. Therefore, the stronger right of the seller should certainly not be nullified. Abu Hanifa responds that construction is intended to be permanent and occurred with the seller's implicit permission (taslit), unlike the pre-emptor who gave no permission. Thus, the seller's right is cut off by the building, analogous to a valid sale (where the buyer's ownership is clear). Ya‘qub later doubted the narration from Abu Hanifa, and Muhammad confirmed the difference of opinion in his book on shuf‘a. The passage illustrates the importance of authorization (taslit) in nullifying the right of return.

Key Terms
تسليط (taslīṭ): Authorization; giving permission or control. Here, the seller implicitly authorized the buyer to use the land, thereby cutting off his right to reclaim.
حق الشفعة (ḥaqq al-shuf‘a): Right of pre-emption – the right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase property sold to a third party. Used here as a comparison.
قيمة (qīmah): Value; the monetary equivalent of the land, to be paid by the buyer instead of returning the land.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), what must the buyer do if he built on land purchased through a fasid sale?
A) Pay the value of the land and keep the building.
B) Demolish the building and return the land to the seller.
C) Keep both the land and building without any payment.
D) Sell the building to a third party.

Key Takeaway
The dispute hinges on whether the seller's implied authorization (taslit) cuts off his right to reclaim land; Abu Hanifa says yes, the Sahibayn say no.

Building and Planting in a Fasid Sale: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on building/planting in fasid sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the buyer's building/planting in a fasid sale, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Authorization (tasleet) from the seller can cut off his right of revocation; Sahibayn analogize with shuf'a to argue otherwise.

This passage from Al-Hidayah presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute. When a buyer in a fasid sale permanently improves the property by building or planting, does the seller retain the right to revoke the sale and reclaim the property? Abu Hanifa says no, based on the idea of authorization; the Sahibayn say yes, drawing an analogy with pre-emption. This lesson dissects their proofs and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the two positions
State the facts: a fasid sale of a house, buyer builds, then seller seeks revocation. Identify Abu Hanifa's ruling (buyer pays value) and Sahibayn's (buyer demolishes and returns).
2
Understand the Sahibayn's analogy with shuf'a
The Sahibayn argue that the seller's right to revoke is stronger than a pre-emptor's right. Since the pre-emptor's weaker right is not nullified by building, the seller's stronger right should a fortiori not be nullified.
This is a qiyas awlawi (a fortiori analogy).
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's counter-argument based on tasleet
Abu Hanifa argues that building is intended for permanence and was done with the seller's authorization (tasleet). This authorization cuts off the seller's right of revocation, just as it would in sale or gift.
The seller authorized the buyer to take possession; this is absent in shuf'a.
4
Note the riwayah issue and the mufta bihi
Ya'qub (Abu Yusuf) narrated Abu Hanifa's view but later doubted. Imam Muhammad confirmed the ikhtilaf. The Urdu commentary states that Abu Hanifa's view is the madhhab (mufta bihi).
Authority weight: zahir riwayah, but with doubt resolved by tarjih.
5
Recognize the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa: buyer keeps property and pays its market value. Under Sahibayn: buyer must demolish or remove the building/plants and return the land.
This difference affects the buyer's financial burden and the property's condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال و من باع دارا بيعا فاسدا فبناها المشترى فعليه قيمتها عند ابي حنيفة رواه يعقوب عنه في الجامع الصغير ثم شک بعد ذالك فى الرواية وقالا ينقض البناء وترد الدار والغرس على هذا الاختلاف لهما ان حق الشفيع اضعف من حق البائع حتى يحتاج فيه الى القضاء ويبطل بالتاخير بخلاف حق البائع ثم اضعف الحقين لا يبطل بالبناء فاقواهما اولى وله ان البنا والغرس مما يقصد به الدوام وقد حصل بتسليط من جهة البائع فينقطع حق الاسترداد كالبيع بخلاف حق الشفيع لانه لم يوجد منه التسليط ولهذا لا يبطل بهبة المشترى وبيعه فكذابنائه وشك يعقوب في حفظه الرواية عن ابي حنيفة وقد نص محمد على الاختلاف في كتاب الشفعة فان حق الشفعة مبنى على انقطاع حق البائع بالبناء وثبوته على الاختلاف
He said: And whoever sells a house in an invalid sale, and the buyer builds on it, then he owes its value according to Abu Hanifa. Ya'qub narrated this from him in al-Jami' al-Saghir, then afterwards he doubted the narration. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: the building is demolished, and the house is returned. And planting is subject to the same disagreement. Their proof is that the right of the pre-emptor is weaker than the right of the seller, to the extent that it requires a court order and is nullified by delay, unlike the seller's right. Then the weaker of the two rights is not nullified by building, so the stronger one is more worthy. And his (Abu Hanifa's) proof is that building and planting are intended for permanence, and they occurred with authorization from the seller, so the right of revocation is cut off, like in the case of a sale, unlike the right of the pre-emptor because no authorization came from him. For this reason, it is not nullified by the buyer's gift or sale, and similarly by his building. And Ya'qub doubted his preservation of the narration from Abu Hanifa, and Muhammad explicitly stated the disagreement in Kitab al-Shuf'a, for the right of pre-emption is based on whether the seller's right is cut off by building, and its establishment is subject to disagreement.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage from Al-Hidayah presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a fasid (invalid) sale. When a buyer, in a fasid sale, builds a structure or plants trees on the purchased property, can the seller revoke the sale and demand the property back, or is the buyer entitled to keep the property in exchange for compensation? The text presents the case: if someone sells a house in a fasid sale and the buyer builds on it, Abu Hanifa rules that the buyer must pay the value of the house (i.e., its market price) and may keep the house. This ruling is narrated by Ya'qub (Imam Abu Yusuf) from Abu Hanifa in al-Jami' al-Saghir, but later Abu Yusuf doubted whether he heard it correctly. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that the buyer must demolish the building and return the house to the seller. The same dispute applies to planting trees on land. The Sahibayn's proof is based on an analogy with shuf'a (pre-emption). They argue that the right of a pre-emptor is weaker than the seller's right of revocation because the pre-emptor needs a court order to enforce his right and loses it by delay, while the seller does not. Despite this weakness, the pre-emptor's right is not nullified when the buyer builds on the property; the buyer must still demolish and hand over the property to the pre-emptor. Therefore, the seller's stronger right should a fortiori not be nullified by the buyer's building. Abu Hanifa's proof is based on the nature of building and planting, which are intended for permanence, and the fact that the buyer carried out these actions with the seller's authorization (since the seller consented to the sale and delivered possession). This authorization cuts off the seller's right of revocation, just as it would be cut off if the buyer sold the property to a third party or gave it as a gift. The difference with shuf'a is that the pre-emptor never authorized the buyer's actions; the buyer's possession came from the seller, not the pre-emptor. Regarding the doubt of Ya'qub, Imam Muhammad later confirmed the disagreement explicitly in his book Kitab al-Shuf'a, stating that the matter is subject to ikhtilaf. The Urdu commentary clarifies that despite Ya'qub's doubt, the madhhab of Abu Hanifa is clear: the seller cannot take back the property; the buyer must pay its value.

Key Terms
بيع فاسد (bay' fasid): A sale that is invalid due to a deficiency in a condition or pillar, but not amounting to batil; possession under such a sale is considered akin to ghasp (usurpation).
حق الاسترداد (haqq al-istirdad): The seller's right to revoke the sale and reclaim the property in a fasid sale before any change occurs.
تسليط (tasleet): Authorization or empowerment given by the seller to the buyer to take possession and act on the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a buyer in a fasid sale builds a house on the purchased land, what is the ruling?
A) The buyer must demolish the building and return the land to the seller.
B) The buyer may keep the land and must pay its market value to the seller.
C) The buyer must pay double the value of the land.
D) The seller can take back the land for free.

Key Takeaway
In a fasid sale, if the buyer builds or plants with the seller's authorization, Abu Hanifa's relied-upon ruling requires the buyer to pay the property's value, cutting off the seller's right of revocation; the Sahibayn require demolition, but this is not the mufta bihi.

Effect of Buyer's Construction on Seller's Right of Return in Fasid Sale
Intra-Hanafi dispute on buyer's improvements extinguishing seller's right of return in fasid sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Hanifah and the Sahibayn regarding the effect of buyer's improvements on the seller's right of return (istirdad) in a fasid sale, and apply the Hanafi tarjih to determine the operative ruling.

Core Idea
Buyer's improvements extinguish seller's right to reclaim if seller enabled possession, but not pre-emptor's right.

This passage examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: does a buyer's construction or planting on property acquired through a fasid (voidable) sale eliminate the seller's right to reclaim that property? Imam Abu Hanifah holds that the seller's right is replaced by a claim for the market price, while the two companions (Sahibayn) insist the buyer must demolish and return the land. The text resolves the issue by clarifying the underlying principle of 'power (tamkin)' and confirms that the relied-upon position is Imam Abu Hanifah's.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute and parties
Recognize that the disagreement is between Imam Abu Hanifah and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) on whether buyer's improvements extinguish the seller's right of return (istirdad) in a fasid sale.
2
Analyze the Sahibayn's proof via analogy with shuf'ah
The Sahibayn argue by qiyas: if a weaker right (shuf'ah) is not extinguished by improvements, then the stronger right (istirdad) should also remain. Understand their a fortiori reasoning.
shuf'ah is weaker because it depends on court order, is non-inheritable, and lapses by delay.
3
Analyze Imam Abu Hanifah's proof based on tamkin
Imam Abu Hanifah argues that the seller's permission to possess gave the buyer the power to build; therefore, the buyer's disposal (building) is attributed to the seller, extinguishing istirdad, similar to sale or gift.
The pre-emptor never gave such power, so shuf'ah remains.
4
Examine the cross-reference to Kitab al-Shuf'ah
The passage cites a parallel dispute: if land is bought fasid and improved, Imam Abu Hanifah grants shuf'ah, proving istirdad is extinguished; Sahibayn deny shuf'ah, consistent with their view that istirdad remains.
Shuf'ah and istirdad cannot coexist in the same property.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The author of al-Hidayah affirms Imam Abu Hanifah's view as the relied-upon position. The buyer pays the market price and keeps the improvements.
This prevents wasteful demolition and aligns with the principle of tamkin.

پر ارض مبیعہ کی قیمت واجب ہوتی ہے ) سنا ہے یا نہیں سنا اور امام محمد سے یہ روایت بیان کی یا بیان نہیں کی حضرت مام ابو حنیفہ کے مذہب میں کوئی شبہ نہیں ہے ان کا مذہب بالیقین یہی ہے کہ اگر بیع فاسد کے طور پر کوئی زمین خرید کر اس میں مشتری نے عمارت بنا ڈالی یا درخت لگا دیئے تو اس کی وجہ سے بائع کا حق استرداد ساقط ہو جائے گا اور مشتری پر ارض مبیعہ کی قیمت واجب ہوگی اور س پر کہ یہ مذکورہ حکم حضرت امام ابو حنیفہ کامذہب ہے دلیل یہ ہے کہ حضرت امام محمد نے کتاب الشفعہ میں حضرت امام صاحب اور صاحبین کے درمیان ایسے مسئلہ میں اختلاف کی تصریح کی ہے جو مذکورہ مسئلہ میں اختلاف پر مبنی ہے چنانچہ کتاب الشفعہ میں مذکور ہے کہ اگر کسی نے بیع فاسد کے طور پر کوئی قطعہ ارض خرید کر اس میں تعمیر نو کرڈالی یا درخت لگا دیئے تو حضرت امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک اس قطعہ ارض میں شفیع کو حق شفعہ حاصل ہے یعنی شفیع کو یہ حق ہے کہ وہ قاضی کی طرف مراجعت کر کے اس ارض مشفوعہ کو س گلے اور مشتری پر واجب ہے کہ وہ عمارت توڑ کر اور درخت اکھاڑ کر یہ زمین شفیع کے حوالہ کر دے اور صاحبین کے نزدیک اس صورت میں شفیع کو کوئی حق شفعہ حاصل نہ ہوگا امام صاحب اور صاحبین کے درمیان یہ اختلاف اس پر مبنی ہے کہ مشتری کے عمارت بنانے اور درخت لگانے سے بائع کا حق استرداد ( مبیع واپس لینے کا حق ) ساقط ہو جاتا ہے یا نہیں پس امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک چونکہ شفیع کے لئے حق شفعہ ثابت ہے اس لئے ان کے نزدیک بائع کا حق استرداد ساقط ہو گیا ہے کیونکہ یہ دونوں باتیں جمع نہیں ہو سکتیں کہ شفیع کے لئے حق شفعہ بھی ثابت ہو اور بائع کا حق استرداد بھی باقی رہے کیونکہ حق شفعہ اسی صورت میں ثابت ہوتا ہے جبکہ بائع کا وجود ہو اور بیع اس صورت میں موجود رہتی ہے جبکہ بائع کا حق استرداد ساقط ہو جائے پس مذکورہ مسئلہ میں شفیع کے لئے حق شفعہ کا ثابت ہونا اس بات کی دلیل ہے کہ بائع کا حق استرداد ساقط ہو گیا ہے اور جب بائع کا حق استرداد ساقط ہو گیا تو بائع مشتری سے بیع کی قیمت لے گا نہ کہ ثمن کیونکہ بیع فاسد کی صورت میں مشتری پر میچ کی قیمت واجب ہوتی ہے شمن واجب نہیں ہوتا بہر حال اس تفصیل سے معلوم ہو گیا کہ امام صاحب کا مذہب یہی ہے کہ مشتری کی عمارت بنانے اور درخت لگانے سے بائع کا مبیع واپس لینے کا حق ساقط ہو جائے اور مشتری پر مبیع کی قیمت واجب ہو۔ اور صاحبین کے نزدیک چونکہ شفیع کے لئے مذکورہ صورت میں حق شفعہ حاصل نہیں ہوتا اس لئے معلوم ہوا کہ ان کے نزدیک بائع کا حق استرداد ساقط نہیں ہوا بلکہ مشتری پر واجب ہے کہ وہ اپنی تعمیر کردہ عمارت کو توڑ کر اور درخت اکھاڑ کر مبیع بائع کو سپرد کرے۔
Returning the house and uprooting the trees and returning the land is obligatory. The author of al-Hidayah said: Imam Muhammad narrated the statement of Imam Abu Hanifah from Imam Abu Yusuf, and Imam Abu Yusuf narrated it from Imam Abu Hanifah in al-Jami' al-Saghir, but thereafter Imam Abu Yusuf began to doubt his narration as to whether he had heard it from Imam Abu Hanifah or not. However, Imam Abu Hanifah's madhhab is that the seller cannot take back the sold item; rather, its price is due on the buyer. So even though Abu Yusuf doubted his narration, there is no doubt about Imam Abu Hanifah's madhhab. The proof of the two companions (Sahibayn) is that if someone bought a house and built a structure in it, or bought land and planted trees in it, and someone else has a right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) in that house or land, then the buyer's construction and planting do not extinguish the pre-emptor's right; rather, the buyer must demolish the structure and uproot the trees and return the house or land to the pre-emptor. Even though the pre-emptor's right is weaker than the seller's right of return because the pre-emptor needs a court order or the buyer's consent to obtain his right, and if he delays claiming his right it becomes void, and if he dies his right is not inherited by his heirs. In contrast, in a fasid sale, the seller's right of return is not dependent on a court order or the buyer's consent, nor does delay void it, and if the seller dies his right passes to his heirs. So since the pre-emptor's right, which is weaker, is not voided by the buyer's construction and planting, but the buyer must demolish and uproot and hand over the property to the pre-emptor, then the seller's right of return, which is stronger, is even more certainly not voided; rather, in that case it is even more obligatory for the buyer to demolish the structure and uproot the trees and return the property to the seller. The proof of Imam Abu Hanifah is that the buyer's intention in building and planting is to keep them, not to demolish and uproot them. Moreover, the buyer's ability to build and plant was given by the seller in that the buyer took possession of the sold item with the seller's permission. Every disposal (tasarruf) by the buyer that results from the seller's granting of power extinguishes the seller's right to reclaim. For example, if the buyer sells the item to someone else or gives it as a gift and delivers it, the seller's right of return is extinguished. Similarly, by the buyer's constructing in the purchased house or planting trees in the purchased land, the seller's right of return is extinguished. However, the pre-emptor's right is not voided by the buyer's construction and planting, even though the pre-emptor's right is weaker. The reason is that the buyer did not obtain the power of disposal from the pre-emptor. Since the buyer did not obtain power from the pre-emptor, the buyer's disposal does not void the pre-emptor's right. And since the pre-emptor's right is not voided, the pre-emptor will be given his right by forcing the buyer to demolish and uproot. Because the pre-emptor's right is not voided by the buyer's disposal, if the buyer gave the land as a gift and delivered it to the donee, or sold it, the pre-emptor's right is not voided; rather, in case of gift the pre-emptor takes the gifted land from the donee, and in case of sale he takes it from the second buyer. So just as the pre-emptor's right is not voided by gift or sale, similarly it is not voided by construction and planting. The author of al-Hidayah again emphasized that Imam Abu Yusuf doubted his narration from Imam Abu Hanifah, i.e., he was uncertain whether he had heard this statement from him or not, and whether he related it from Imam Muhammad or not. But there is no doubt about Imam Abu Hanifah's madhhab; his madhhab with certainty is that if someone buys land by fasid sale and then builds a structure or plants trees on it, the seller's right of return is extinguished, and the buyer is liable for the price of the land. Evidence that this is Imam Abu Hanifah's madhhab is that Imam Muhammad in Kitab al-Shuf'ah recorded a difference between Imam Abu Hanifah and the Sahibayn in a similar issue that is based on the same point. Thus in Kitab al-Shuf'ah it is mentioned that if someone buys a plot of land by fasid sale and then builds on it or plants trees, according to Imam Abu Hanifah the pre-emptor has a right of shuf'ah in that plot, meaning the pre-emptor can approach the judge and take the land, and the buyer must demolish the structure and uproot the trees and hand over the land to the pre-emptor. According to the Sahibayn, the pre-emptor has no right in this case. This difference between Imam Abu Hanifah and the Sahibayn is based on whether the buyer's construction and planting extinguish the seller's right of return or not. So since Imam Abu Hanifah affirms the pre-emptor's right, in his view the seller's right of return is extinguished, because these two cannot coexist: that the pre-emptor's right is affirmed while the seller's right of return remains. For the pre-emptor's right is only affirmed when the sale exists, and the sale exists only when the seller's right of return is extinguished. Thus, in this case the affirmation of the pre-emptor's right is proof that the seller's right of return is extinguished. And when the seller's right of return is extinguished, the seller takes the price (qimat) from the buyer, not the specific price (thaman), because in a fasid sale the buyer owes the price (qimat), not the specific price. In any case, from this detail it becomes clear that Imam Abu Hanifah's madhhab is that the buyer's construction and planting extinguish the seller's right to reclaim, and the buyer owes the price of the sold item. According to the Sahibayn, since in this case the pre-emptor has no right, it is known that in their view the seller's right of return is not extinguished; rather, it is obligatory for the buyer to demolish his construction and uproot the trees and hand over the sold item to the seller.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute (ikhtilaf) within the Hanafi school regarding the effect of the buyer's improvements (building or planting) on the seller's right to reclaim the sold item in a fasid (voidable) sale. The dispute is between Imam Abu Hanifah and the two companions (Sahibayn: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). The text clarifies a narration issue: Imam Abu Yusuf transmitted Imam Abu Hanifah's view in al-Jami' al-Saghir but later doubted whether he had actually heard it. However, the text confirms that Imam Abu Hanifah's madhhab is certain: the buyer's construction/planting extinguishes the seller's right to reclaim the property, and the buyer must pay the market price (qimat) instead of returning the specific item. The Sahibayn argue by analogy (qiyas) with the case of shuf'ah (pre-emption): if a pre-emptor's right (which is weaker) is not extinguished by the buyer's improvements, then the seller's stronger right should also not be extinguished. They derive the weakness of the pre-emptor's right from its dependence on court order and its non-inheritability. Imam Abu Hanifah responds by distinguishing the two cases: the buyer's power to build was given by the seller (through permission to take possession), so the seller's right is extinguished, similar to when the buyer sells or gives away the property. The pre-emptor did not grant any power to the buyer, so his right remains. The text then reinforces this by citing a parallel dispute in Kitab al-Shuf'ah: if the land was purchased fasid and then improved, Imam Abu Hanifah still grants shuf'ah (pre-emption) to the pre-emptor, proving that in his view the seller's right has been replaced by a price obligation; otherwise shuf'ah would not apply. The Sahibayn deny shuf'ah in that case, consistent with their view that the seller still owns the land. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is that of Imam Abu Hanifah, as affirmed by the author of al-Hidayah and by this commentary.

Key Terms
حق استرداد (haqq al-istirdād): The seller's right to reclaim the sold item (mabī') in a fasid sale when the contract is voidable.
بیع فاسد (bay' fāsid): A voidable sale where the contract is defective in a way that can be corrected (e.g., missing condition), but possession and some effects may be valid. The contract is not void (bātil) but is deficient.
شفعہ (shuf'ah): The right of pre-emption: a co-owner or neighbor has the right to purchase a sold property by replacing the buyer under certain conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifah, what happens when a buyer builds a house on land purchased in a fasid sale?
A) The seller's right to reclaim the land is extinguished, and the buyer must pay the market price.
B) The buyer must demolish the house and return the land to the seller.
C) The sale becomes valid automatically.
D) The buyer keeps the land and pays nothing.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when a buyer improves property acquired through a fasid sale, the seller's right to reclaim is extinguished if the seller enabled possession; the buyer pays the market price.

Ruling on Profit from a Fasid Sale: 'Ayn vs Dayn
Ruling on profit from fasid sale with talil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on profit from reselling an item bought in fasid sale and explain the distinction between 'ayn and dayn in the transmission of taint (khubth).

Core Idea
Taint from fasid sale attaches only to profit from specific chattels ('ayn); profit from fungible currency (dayn) remains halal.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling from the Hanafi school: when a person buys an item in a voidable (fasid) sale and later resells it at a profit, must that profit be given in charity? The answer depends on whether the item is a specific chattel or fungible currency. The reasoning reveals a key distinction in Hanafi transactional law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act: purchasing a slave girl in a fasid sale, taking possession, then reselling her at a profit. The ruling: the profit must be given in charity (wajib).
The fasid sale is voidable, not void, so possession and subsequent sale have legal effect.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is a rational distinction (talil) based on the nature of the item. The text contrasts the slave girl (specific chattel) with dirhams/dinars (fungible currency).
The dalil is not a primary text (Quran/hadith) but a legal reasoning (qiyas/talil) consistent with Hanafi usul.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Because the slave girl is an 'ayn (specific chattel), the fasid contract attaches to her specific identity, and the taint (khubth) transfers to any profit derived from her resale. For dirhams/dinars, they are fungible, so the contract does not attach to specific coins, and taint does not carry over to subsequent profits from the price.
The key term is 'ta'ayyun' (specification): the item's specificity makes it a carrier of khubth.
4
Apply the farq (distinction)
Apply the distinction to the second scenario: the first seller uses the price money (dirhams/dinars) to trade and makes a profit. That profit is halal because the thaman (price) is fungible and not tainted.
The ruling is specific to khubth arising from fasād (voidability), not from other prohibitions like riba.
5
Extend to analogous cases
Consider modern examples: buying a car in a fasid sale (e.g., missing condition) and reselling it. The car is an 'ayn, so profit must be given in charity. Buying shares (fungible) in a fasid sale? Profit from resale may be treated differently.
The distinction helps evaluate similar cases in contemporary transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باندی کو بیع فاسد سے خریدا دونوں نے شمن اور مبیع پر قبضہ کرلیا پھر مشتری نے باندی کو فروخت کر دیا اور اس سے نفع اٹھایا اس نفع کو صدقہ کر دے اور بائع اول کا شمن میں نفع حلال ہے
قال زمن اشترى جارية بيعا فاسدا وتقابضها فيا عها وربح فيها تصدق بالربح ويطيب للبائع ماربح في الثمن والمفرق ان الجارية مما يتعين فيتعلق العقد بها فيتمكن الخبث في الربح والدراهم والدنا نيز لاتتعينان في العقود فلم يتعلق العقد الثانى بعينها فلم يتمكن الخبث فلا يجب التصدق وهذا في الخبث الذي سببه فساد
A slave girl was bought with a fasid sale, both exchanged the price and the sold item, then the buyer sold the slave girl and made a profit. He should give that profit in charity. And the profit in the price is halal for the first seller. He said: If someone buys a slave girl in a fasid sale and they exchange possession, then he sells her and makes a profit from her, he gives that profit in charity. And the profit the [first] seller made in the price is lawful. The difference is that the slave girl is an 'ayn (specific chattel), so the contract attaches to her, and thus taint (khubth) attaches to the profit. As for dirhams and dinars, they are not specific in contracts, so the second contract does not attach to the specific item, so taint does not attach, so charity is not obligatory. This concerns taint that arises from fasād.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling regarding the profit obtained from reselling a slave girl that was purchased in a fasid (voidable) sale. The ruling is from the matn (likely al-Hidayah or a similar work) and is explained by the commentator. The situation: A customer buys a slave girl in a fasid sale – for example, a sale with a defect that makes it voidable. Both parties exchange possession (the buyer takes the slave girl, the seller takes the price). Then the buyer sells that slave girl to a third party and makes a profit. The ruling: the buyer must give that profit in charity (sadaqah) because it is tainted (khubth) due to the fasid nature of the first sale. However, the profit that the original seller makes in the price (thaman) – i.e., if the seller later uses the money he received to trade and profit – that profit is halal for the seller and does not need to be given in charity. The reasoning (al-farq) provided in the Arabic text explains the difference: The slave girl is a specific chattel ('ayn): contracts attach to the specific item, so the taint (khubth) from the fasid sale attaches to the profit generated from her sale. Dirhams and dinars (currency), on the other hand, are not specific in contracts; they are considered fungible (mithliyyat). Therefore, the second contract (the resale by the buyer) does not attach to the specific coins, so the taint does not attach to any profit the original seller makes from using the price money. Hence, the seller's profit is not tainted and is halal. This ruling is specifically about taint that arises from fasād (voidability), not from other forms of prohibition (like riba or gharar).

Key Terms
بیع فاسد (bay' fāsid): A voidable sale contract that is defective but not void; possession and some obligations are valid, but the contract is subject to annulment.
ربح (ribḥ): Profit gained from reselling the purchased item. In this context, the profit from the slave girl is tainted and must be given in charity.
خبث (khubth): Taint or impurity that affects a transaction due to its unlawfulness or defect. Here, it is the legal impurity arising from the fasid sale that attaches to the profit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the reason for the difference in ruling between the buyer's profit and the seller's profit?
A) The buyer is the one who committed the fasid sale; the seller is innocent.
B) The slave girl is a specific chattel, so taint attaches to its profit; dirhams/dinars are fungible, so taint does not carry over.
C) The seller's profit is larger, so it is exempt.
D) The buyer sold the slave girl before taking possession, invalidating the taint.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, taint from a fasid sale attaches only to profit from specific chattels ('ayn); profit from fungible currency (dayn) remains halal.
