The Effect of Buyer's Improvements on the Seller's Right to Reclaim in a Fasid Sale: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether buyer's building cancels seller's right to reclaim in fasid sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether the buyer's improvement on fasid land cancels the seller's right to reclaim, and apply the evidence from Kitab al-Shuf'a to determine the ruling.

Core Idea
The buyer's building or planting on fasid land cancels the seller's right to reclaim according to Abu Hanifa; Sahibayn disagree.

This passage addresses a key intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of the buyer's improvements on land acquired through a fasid (defective) sale. The central question is whether the seller's right to reclaim (haqq al-istirdad) is extinguished when the buyer has added value through construction or cultivation. The disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two disciples (Sahibayn) has significant practical implications for the right of pre-emption (shuf'a) as well.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the disputed ruling
Identify the act: the buyer's construction or planting on land purchased through a fasid sale. The disputed ruling is whether this cancels the seller's right to reclaim the land.
2
State the Hanafi position of Imam Abu Hanifa
According to Abu Hanifa, the seller's right to reclaim is cancelled, and the buyer owes the market price of the land, not the contracted price (thaman).
3
State the position of the Sahibayn
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the seller's right to reclaim remains. The buyer must remove the improvements and return the land to the seller.
4
Examine the proof from Kitab al-Shuf'a
The proof is based on the authority of the pre-emptor (shafi'). If the right of pre-emption is established for the shafi', it implies that the seller's right to reclaim is cancelled, because the two rights cannot coexist. The pre-emptor's right exists only when the seller has ownership, which occurs when the seller's right to reclaim is cancelled.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: if the buyer improves the land, according to Abu Hanifa the seller cannot reclaim; according to Sahibayn, the seller can reclaim after removal of improvements. This also affects pre-emption: Abu Hanifa grants the shafi' the right, while Sahibayn deny it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حضرت امام ابو یوسف نے حضرت امام ابو حنیفہ سے اپنی روایت کرنے میں شک کیا ہے یعنی اس میں شبہ ہے کہ امام ابو حنیفہ ان کا یہ قول ( کہ مشتری کے عمارت بنانے اور درخت لگانے سے بائع کا حق استرداد ساقط ہو جاتا ہے اور مشتری پر ارض مبیعہ کی قیمت واجب ہوتی ہے ) سنا ہے یا نہیں سنا اور امام محمد سے یہ روایت بیان کی یا بیان نہیں کی حضرت مام ابو حنیفہ کے مذہب میں کوئی شبہ نہیں ہے ان کا مذہب بالیقین یہی ہے کہ اگر بیع فاسد کے طور پر کوئی زمین خرید کر اس میں مشتری نے عمارت بنا ڈالی یا درخت لگا دیئے تو اس کی وجہ سے بائع کا حق استرداد ساقط ہو جائے گا اور مشتری پر ارض مبیعہ کی قیمت واجب ہوگی اور س پر کہ یہ مذکورہ حکم حضرت امام ابو حنیفہ کامذہب ہے دلیل یہ ہے کہ حضرت امام محمد نے کتاب الشفعہ میں حضرت امام صاحب اور صاحبین کے درمیان ایسے مسئلہ میں اختلاف کی تصریح کی ہے جو مذکورہ مسئلہ میں اختلاف پر مبنی ہے چنانچہ کتاب الشفعہ میں مذکور ہے کہ اگر کسی نے بیع فاسد کے طور پر کوئی قطعہ ارض خرید کر اس میں تعمیر نو کرڈالی یا درخت لگا دیئے تو حضرت امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک اس قطعہ ارض میں شفیع کو حق شفعہ حاصل ہے یعنی شفیع کو یہ حق ہے کہ وہ قاضی کی طرف مراجعت کر کے اس ارض مشفوعہ کوس گلے اور مشتری پر واجب ہے کہ وہ عمارت تو ڑ کر اور درخت اکھاڑ کر یہ زمین شفیع کے حوالہ کر دے اور صاحبین کے نزدیک اس صورت میں شفیع کو کوئی حق شفعہ حاصل نہ ہوگا امام صاحب اور صاحبین کے درمیان یہ اختلاف اس پر مبنی ہے کہ مشتری کے عمارت بنانے اور درخت لگانے سے بائع کا حق استرداد ( مبیع واپس لینے کا حق ) ساقط ہو جاتا ہے یا نہیں پس امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک چونکہ شفیع کے لئے حق شفعہ ثابت ہے اس لئے ان کے نزدیک بائع کا حق استرداد ساقط ہو گیا ہے کیونکہ یہ دونوں باتیں جمع نہیں ہو سکتیں کہ شفیع کے لئے حق شفعہ بھی ثابت ہو اور بائع کا حق استرداد بھی باقی رہے کیونکہ حق شفعہ اسی صورت میں ثابت ہوتا ہے جبکہ بائع کا وجود ہو اور بیچ اس صورت میں موجود رہتی ہے جبکہ بائع کا حق استرداد ساقط ہو جائے پس مذکورہ مسئلہ میں شفیع کے لئے حق شفعہ کا ثابت ہونا اس بات کی دلیل ہے کہ بائع کا حق استرداد ساقط ہو گیا ہے اور جب بائع کا حق استرداد ساقط ہو گیا تو بائع مشتری سے بیع کی قیمت لے گا نہ کہ ثمن کیونکہ بیچ فاسد کی صورت میں مشتری پر میچ کی قیمت واجب ہوتی ہے شمن واجب نہیں ہوتا بہر حال اس تفصیل سے معلوم ہو گیا کہ امام صاحب کا مذہب یہی ہے کہ مشتری کی عمارت بنانے اور درخت لگانے سے بائع کا مبیع واپس لینے کا حق ساقط ہو جائے اور مشتری پر مبیع کی قیمت واجب ہو۔ اور صاحبین کے نزدیک چونکہ شفیع کے لئے مذکورہ صورت میں حق شفعہ حاصل نہیں ہوتا اس لئے معلوم ہوا کہ ان کے نزدیک بائع کا حق استرداد ساقط نہیں ہوا بلکہ مشتری پر واجب ہے کہ وہ اپنی تعمیر کردہ عمارت کو توڑ کر اور درخت اکھاڑ کر مبیع بائع کو سپرد کرے۔
Imam Abu Yusuf had doubt in his narration from Imam Abu Hanifa, meaning there is uncertainty whether Imam Abu Hanifa heard this statement (that the buyer's building and planting trees cancels the seller's right to reclaim and the buyer owes the price of the land) or not, and whether he narrated it from Imam Muhammad or not. There is no doubt in the madhhab of Imam Abu Hanifa; his madhhab with certainty is that if someone buys land through a fasid sale and then builds or plants trees, the seller's right to reclaim is cancelled and the buyer owes the price of the land. The evidence for this is that Imam Muhammad in Kitab al-Shuf'a explicitly mentioned a disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on an issue based on this disagreement. It is mentioned in Kitab al-Shuf'a that if someone buys a piece of land through a fasid sale and then builds or plants trees, according to Imam Abu Hanifa the pre-emptor has the right of pre-emption, i.e., he can approach the judge to take the land, and the buyer must demolish the building and uproot the trees and hand over the land to the pre-emptor. According to the Sahibayn, the pre-emptor has no right of pre-emption in this case. This disagreement between Imam and the Sahibayn is based on whether the buyer's building and planting cancels the seller's right to reclaim. Since according to Imam Abu Hanifa the pre-emptor's right is established, his seller's right of reclaim is cancelled, because these two cannot coexist: that the pre-emptor's right be established while the seller's right to reclaim remains, because pre-emption is only established when the seller exists, and the sale exists only when the seller's right to reclaim is cancelled. Therefore, in the mentioned issue, the establishment of the pre-emptor's right is evidence that the seller's right to reclaim is cancelled. And when the seller's right to reclaim is cancelled, the seller takes the price from the buyer, not the thaman, because in a fasid sale the buyer owes the price, not the thaman. In any case, from this detail it is known that Imam's madhhab is that the buyer's building and planting cancels the seller's right to reclaim, and the buyer owes the price. According to the Sahibayn, since the pre-emptor does not have the right of pre-emption in this case, it is understood that according to them the seller's right to reclaim is not cancelled; rather, the buyer is obliged to demolish his building and uproot the trees and hand over the sold land to the seller.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of the buyer's construction or plantation on land purchased through a fasid (defective) sale. The core issue is whether the seller's right to reclaim the land (haqq al-istirdād) is cancelled when the buyer has made improvements. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the right is cancelled, and the buyer is liable for the market price of the land, not the contracted price. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree, holding that the seller's right remains and the buyer must return the land after removing improvements. The commentator provides a proof from Kitab al-Shuf'a: if land is bought through a fasid sale and improvements are made, Imam Abu Hanifa grants the pre-emptor (shafi') the right of pre-emption, because the seller's right to reclaim is cancelled. The Sahibayn deny pre-emption, implying the seller's right remains. The reasoning is that pre-emption requires the seller to have ownership, which exists only when the seller's right to reclaim is cancelled. Thus, the establishment of pre-emption under Imam Abu Hanifa is evidence of cancellation. The commentator also notes that Abu Yusuf doubted whether he correctly narrated this ruling from Abu Hanifa, but the ruling is firmly attributed to Abu Hanifa through cross-referencing. The mufta bihi position is not explicitly stated here, but the text emphasizes that Imam Abu Hanifa's position is certain.

Key Terms
حق استرداد (haqq al-istirdad): The seller's right to take back the sold item in a fasid sale after the contract, due to the sale being defective.
شفعه (shuf'a): Pre-emption: the right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase property that has been sold to a third party under certain conditions.
بیع فاسد (bay' fasid): A sale that is defective in some aspect (e.g., lack of capacity, unknown price) but not completely invalid. Possession and some effects are recognized.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the buyer's building on land bought through a fasid sale, what is the consequence according to Imam Abu Hanifa?
A) The seller's right to reclaim the land is cancelled and the buyer pays the market price.
B) The seller can still reclaim the land after the buyer demolishes the building.
C) The buyer must return the land and also pay compensation for the building.
D) The sale becomes valid and the buyer owns the land without any payment.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when the buyer builds or plants on land from a fasid sale, Imam Abu Hanifa rules that the seller's right to reclaim is cancelled and the buyer pays the price, while the Sahibayn rule that the seller may reclaim after removal of improvements.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
advanced
8 min
Ruling on Profit from a Fasid Sale: Specific vs Non-Specific Items
Ruling explaining the distinction between specific and non-specific items in fasid sales regarding profit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on profit from a fasid sale, distinguish between specific and non-specific items, and apply the rule to determine whether charity is required.

Core Idea
Profit from a specific item in a fasid sale is tainted; profit from currency is pure and halal.

When a sale is fasid (defective), the buyer still acquires a form of ownership (milk fasid). If he later sells the item at a profit, that profit may be impure (khubth) and must be purified by giving it in charity. However, this ruling depends on whether the item is muta'ayyin (specific) or ghayr muta'ayyin (non-specific). This passage from Al-Hidayah explains the distinction using the example of a slave-girl versus dirhams, and clarifies the underlying legal reasoning based on how contracts attach to goods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two cases: (1) the buyer resells the slave-girl at a profit, and (2) the seller uses the price to make a profit. Recognize which party is involved and what they do.
2
State the ruling for each
For the buyer's profit on the slave-girl: he must give that profit in charity. For the seller's profit on the price: it is halal and no charity required.
This is the core ruling of the passage.
3
Understand the key concept: muta'ayyin
Items like a slave-girl or cloth become specific by specification in the contract – they are muta'ayyin. Dirhams and dinars are ghayr muta'ayyin – they are not tied to specific coins unless explicitly agreed otherwise (and even then, the Hanafi position is that they remain non-specific in essence).
The Hanafi position (three Imams) holds that currency is never truly muta'ayyin; Imam Zufar and al-Shafi'i disagree.
4
Trace the legal reasoning: khubth and attachment of contract
A fasid contract attaches to the substance (dhat) of a specific item, so any profit from resale carries khubth (impurity) due to defective ownership. For non-specific items like currency, the contract does not attach to specific coins, so no khubth arises in the profit.
This is a qiyas (analogy) based on the nature of specificity.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi and cross-madhhab ikhtilaf
The three Imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) hold the view explained. Imam Zufar within the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i consider currency to be muta'ayyin, leading to different rulings. The mufta bihi is the majority view.
Practical implication: In fatwa, the majority view is followed.

یہ ذہن نشین کر لیجئے کہ اموال کی دو قسمیں ہیں ایک وہ جو عقود میں متعین کرنے سے متعین ہو جاتے ہیں جیسے دراہم ود نا نیر کے علاوہ اشیاء باندی ، کپڑا وغیرہ۔ چنانچہ اگر متعینہ باندی فروخت کی گئی تو بائع کے لئے اس کی جگہ دوسری باندی سپر د کرنا جائز نہیں ہے۔ دوسری قسم وہ ہے جو عقود میں متعین کرنے سے متعین نہیں ہوتے جیسے دراہم اور دنا نیر چنا نچہ اگر کسی نے کوئی چیز متعینہ دراہم کے عوض خریدی پھر مشتری نے اور دوسرا محبت دونوں کے اندر مؤثر ہوتا ہے ( عنایہ ) اس کی تمہید کے بعد دونوں صورتوں میں فرق ملاحظہ فرمائیے فرق یہ ہے کہ باندی ایسی چیز ہے جو متعین کرنے سے متعین ہو جاتی ہے اور جب باندی متعین ہے تو عقد ثانی اس کی ذات کے ساتھ متعلق ہو گا حالانکہ یہ باندی بیع فاسد کی وجہ سے مشتری کی ملک فاسد تھی پس مشتری کا اس باندی کو بیکر نفع حاصل کرنا ملک فاسد سے نفع حاصل کرنا ہے اور جو نفع
A slave-girl is bought through a fasid sale; both take possession of the price and the sold item. Then the buyer sells the slave-girl and makes a profit. He must give that profit in charity. And the original seller's profit from the price is halal. He (the author) said: Whoever buys a slave-girl through a fasid sale, and they exchange possession, then he sells her and makes profit, he gives the profit in charity. And for the seller, the profit he makes from the price is permissible. The difference is that the slave-girl is a specific item, so the contract attaches to her substance; thus impurity settles in the profit. Dirhams and dinars are not specific in contracts, so the second contract does not attach to their substance, so no impurity settles, and charity is not obligatory. This applies to the impurity caused by the defect of ownership. As for the impurity due to lack of ownership according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, it includes both types, because the contract attaches to specific items in reality and to non-specific items by similitude, in the sense that the soundness of the sold item or the estimation of the price attaches to it. And when ownership is defective, the reality turns into similitude, and similitude descends to similitude of similitude. And similitude is considered, but what is lower than similitude is not considered. Translation: If someone buys a slave-girl through a fasid sale and both take possession—the seller of the price and the buyer of the slave-girl—then the buyer sells the slave-girl and makes profit, it is obligatory to give that profit in charity. But if the seller buys something with the price and makes profit, that profit is permissible for him and charity is not obligatory. Before the difference between these two cases, understand that there are two types of property: those that become specific by being specified in contracts, like items other than dirhams and dinars, e.g., slave-girl, cloth. So if a specific slave-girl is sold, the seller cannot hand over another slave-girl in its place. The second type is that which does not become specific by being specified, like dirhams and dinars. So if someone buys something for specified dirhams, then the buyer wants to give other similar dirhams, the seller cannot refuse; he is forced to accept. This is the madhhab of the three Imams: Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad. As for Imam Zufar and Imam Shafi'i, according to them dirhams and dinars also become specific by specification. So if dirhams are specified in the contract and the seller is given other dirhams, these two Imams hold that the seller can refuse. And if those specified dirhams are destroyed, the sale becomes void, just as if a specific sold item is destroyed. But according to the three Imams, if specified dirhams are destroyed, the sale does not become void. And impurity (khubth) is of two types: one due to defective ownership (fasid milk), the other due to lack of ownership ('adam al-milk). The first affects items that become specific by specification, but not non-specific items. The second affects both types. After this preamble, note the difference: The slave-girl is an item that becomes specific by specification. When the slave-girl is specific, the second contract attaches to her substance, even though she was in the buyer's defective ownership due to the fasid sale. So the buyer's profit from selling her is profit from defective ownership.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with proof on the treatment of profit from a fasid sale, distinguishing between profit derived from a specific commodity (like a slave-girl) and profit derived from currency (dirhams/dinars). The matn states: if one buys a slave-girl through a fasid sale, takes possession, then sells her and makes a profit, he must give that profit in charity. However, if the seller takes the price (thaman) and uses it to buy something else, making a profit, that profit is halal for him and no charity is required. The commentary explains the difference: the slave-girl is muta'ayyin (specific/determined) in the contract; therefore, the first fasid contract attaches to her very substance. When the buyer resells her, the profit is tainted with khubth (impurity) because it arises from a defective ownership (milk fasid). Hence, the profit must be purified by sadaqah. In contrast, dirhams and dinars are ghayr muta'ayyin (non-specific) in contracts; they are considered fungible. The second contract (the seller's use of the price) does not attach to specific coins, so no khubth attaches to the profit. Thus, no charity is required. The passage further clarifies that khubth is of two types: one due to fasid milk (defective ownership) and one due to 'adam al-milk (lack of ownership). The first type only affects specific items; the second affects both. The discussion also mentions an intra-Hanafi difference: the three Imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) hold that dirhams/dinars are not specific, while Imam Zufar and Imam Shafi'i consider them specific. This distinction determines the applicability of the khubth rules. The commentary provides a detailed logical hierarchy: the contract attaches to specific items in reality (haqiqah) and to non-specific items by similitude (shubhah). When ownership is defective, the reality transforms into similitude, and that similitude may descend further, but only the first similitude is considered. Therefore, the profit from the slave-girl (specific) is impure and must be given in charity, whereas the profit from the price (non-specific) is pure and permissible.

Key Terms
متعین (muta'ayyin): A specific/determined item that becomes uniquely identified by specification in a contract; e.g., a particular slave-girl or cloth.
خبث (khubth): Impurity or unlawfulness in profit due to a defective or invalid ownership. It requires purification by giving the profit in charity.
ملک فاسد (milk fasid): Defective ownership where the title is not fully valid due to a defect in the transaction (e.g., fasid sale). The buyer is considered to have ownership but with a legal flaw.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, if a person buys a slave-girl through a fasid sale and then sells her at a profit, what must he do with that profit?
A) Keep the profit as it is lawful because he owned the slave-girl.
B) Give the profit in charity to purify it.
C) Return the profit to the original seller.
D) Destroy the profit as it is completely unlawful.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, profit from a fasid sale of a specific item must be given in charity, but profit from non-specific items like money is halal.

Profit from Fasid Sale: Determinable vs Non-Determinable Items
Explains ruling on profit from fasid sale and distinction between mutayyan and ghayr mutayyan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mutayyan and ghayr mutayyan items and apply the Hanafi ruling on profit from a fasid sale to each category.

Core Idea
Profit from a corrupt sale is tainted only when the item is determinable; currency remains pure.

In this lesson, we examine a key distinction in Hanafi fiqh: whether profit earned from a corrupt (fasid) sale must be given in charity depends on the nature of the item sold. A specific slave-girl carries impurity, but dirhams as fungibles do not. This ruling hinges on whether the contract attaches to the specific essence of the item or only to its genus. We will explore the reasoning of the three imams and the opposing view of Imam Zufar and Imam Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify the two scenarios: profit from fasid sale of a determinable item (slave-girl) must be given in charity; profit from a non-determinable item (dirhams) is halal.
The ruling is based on the nature of the item, not the type of sale.
2
Categorize the item as mutayyan or ghayr mutayyan
Determine whether the item becomes specifically identified by the contract. A slave-girl is mutayyan; dirhams are ghayr mutayyan (according to the three imams).
This classification is contested by Zufar and Shafi'i.
3
Analyze contract attachment
For mutayyan items, the contract attaches to the essence, so impurity (khabth) transfers to profit. For ghayr mutayyan, the contract attaches to the genus, preventing impurity transfer.
The distinction is based on the function of the item: currency as a measure of value.
4
Compare opposing views
Imam Zufar and Shafi'i argue that specification makes dirhams determinable, so profit from them would require charity.
This leads to different rulings on the validity of the sale if specified dirhams are destroyed.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The Hanafi position (three imams) is relied upon: dirhams are non-determinable, profit is halal.
Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah sides with the three imams.

Book of Sales. The ownership is either [due to] impurity for lack of ownership, according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad it includes both types because the contract attaches to that which is determinable in reality and to that which is not determinable in terms of semblance, insofar as it attaches to the safety of the sold item or the estimation of the price. And when ownership is corrupt, reality turns into semblance, and semblance descends to semblance of semblance, and what is considered is the semblance, not that which is lower. Translation: And if someone bought a slave-girl in a corrupt sale, and the seller and buyer took possession, then the buyer sold the slave-girl and made a profit, he should give the profit in charity. And the profit made by the first seller from the price is lawful for him. The difference is that the slave-girl is a determinable thing, so the contract of sale attaches to her essence; thus the impurity of prohibition settles in the profit. As for dirhams and dinars, since they are not determinable in contracts, the second contract does not attach to their essence, and impurity does not settle, so giving in charity is not obligatory. And this distinction is in the impurity whose cause includes both types, because the second contract in a determinable item is real, and in a non-determinable item is by semblance, in the sense that the safety of the sold item or the estimation of the price attaches to it. And when ownership is corrupt, reality transforms into semblance, and semblance descends into semblance of semblance, while the considered one is the semblance, not that which is lower. Explanation: The scenario is that if someone buys a slave-girl in a corrupt sale and both take possession, i.e., the seller took possession of the price and the buyer of the slave-girl, then the buyer sold the slave-girl and made a profit, it is obligatory for the buyer to give the amount of profit in charity. But if the seller bought something with the price and made a profit, that profit is permissible for the seller; it is not obligatory to give it in charity. Before the difference between these two cases, please note that wealth is of two types: one that becomes determinable by specifying in contracts, like items other than dirhams and dinars, e.g., a slave-girl, cloth, etc. So if a specific slave-girl is sold, the seller cannot deliver another slave-girl in her place. The second type is that which does not become determinable by specifying in contracts, like dirhams and dinars. So if someone bought something for specific dirhams, then the buyer wanted to give other dirhams similar to them, the seller has no right to refuse, but the seller will be forced to accept those dirhams that the buyer wants to give. This is the madhhab of the three imams of the Hanafis: Imam Abu Hanifa, Imam Abu Yusuf, and Imam Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them). But according to Imam Zufar and Imam Shafi'i, dirhams and dinars become determinable by specifying. And if in a contract of sale, specified dirhams are made the price, and the seller is given other than them, then according to these two, the seller can refuse to accept them. And if those specified dirhams are destroyed, then according to them the sale becomes void, just as a sale becomes void when a specified sold item is destroyed. But according to the three imams, if specified dirhams are destroyed, the sale does not become void. And impurity (impurity of prohibition) is of two types: one is the impurity that occurs due to corrupt ownership, and the second is the impurity that occurs due to lack of ownership. The first impurity only affects those things that become determinable by specifying, but it does not affect things that do not become determinable by specifying. The second impurity affects both. (From Inayah) After this preamble, observe the difference between the two cases: The difference is that the slave-girl is something that becomes determinable by specifying, and when the slave-girl is determinable, the second contract attaches to her essence, though this slave-girl was in the corrupt ownership of the buyer due to the corrupt sale. So the buyer's making a profit by selling this slave-girl is profit from corrupt ownership, and profit obtained from corrupt ownership has the impurity of prohibition effective and attached to it. And when impurity is found in a thing, it is obligatory to give it in charity; using it oneself is not permissible. Therefore, it is obligatory for the buyer to give the profit he made by selling the slave-girl in charity. And as for dirhams and dinars, according to us, since they are not determinable by specifying, the second contract does not attach to the essence of those dirhams that the seller received in exchange for the slave-girl. So this purchase does not attach to the essence of those dirhams, because dirhams and dinars are not determinable despite specifying. And when this contract, i.e., the seller's purchase of something, did not attach to the essence of those dirhams that he received in exchange for the slave-girl, then the profit obtained through this contract will not have impurity, and when there is no impurity in that profit, it is not obligatory to give it in charity. Sahib al-Hidayah states: The distinction between what is determinable and what is not determinable is in that impurity whose cause is the corruption of ownership. That is, if impurity arises in a determinable thing due to corruption of ownership, then it is not permissible for him; rather, it is obligatory to give it in charity. And if impurity arises in a non-determinable thing due to corruption of ownership, then it is permissible; it is not obligatory to give it in charity, as has passed in the previous detail.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute from the commentary on al-Hidayah regarding the ruling on profit from a fasid (corrupt) sale. The base ruling: if one buys a slave-girl in a fasid sale and then sells her at a profit, the profit must be given in charity because it is tainted by the impurity (khabth) of the fasid ownership. However, if the original seller takes the price (dirhams) and uses it to buy something else, any profit from that is halal for him. The commentator explains the key distinction: slave-girls and other specific items are mutayyan (determinable by specification in the contract), so the contract attaches to their exact essence, and the impurity of the fasid ownership transfers to any profit derived from them. Dirhams and dinars, in the Hanafi view of the three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad), are ghayr mutayyan (non-determinable); they are fungibles and the contract does not attach to specific coins but to the genus. Therefore, the impurity does not transfer. The commentator then introduces an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Zufar and Imam Shafi'i hold that dirhams are determinable, so the same ruling would apply to them. The passage also distinguishes two types of khabth: one due to fasid milk (corrupt ownership) which only affects determinable items, and one due to ‘adam al-milk (lack of ownership, e.g., ghasp) which affects both. This distinction is crucial for later cases. The commentator follows the author's order, first presenting the ruling, then the reasoning, then the dispute, and finally the general principle.

Key Terms
متعین (mutayyan): An item that becomes specifically identified by the contract, such that the seller cannot substitute it with another of the same type. E.g., a specific slave-girl.
غیر متعین (ghayr mutayyan): An item that is not specifically identified by the contract; its identity is by type and quantity. The seller can deliver any item of that type and quantity.
خبث (khabth): Legal impurity or unlawfulness in property or profit due to a defect in ownership (fasid milk) or lack of ownership (adam al-milk). It necessitates purification through charity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab (three imams), if someone buys a slave-girl in a fasid sale and then sells her at a profit, what must they do with the profit?
A) Keep it as halal profit
B) Give it in charity
C) Return it to the original seller
D) Destroy it

Key Takeaway
Profit from a corrupt sale is tainted (requires charity) only when the sold item is determinable (mutayyan); profit from non-determinable goods (mithliyyat) like currency is halal.

Profit from Usurped Property: Majority View vs. Abu Yusuf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on profit from ghasp after damān.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi majority position on profit from usurped property after damān and evaluate Abu Yusuf's counter-argument using the concepts of real and semblance attachment.

Core Idea
Profit from usurped property remains tainted even after compensation; the contract's attachment carries the impurity.

The second type of khabth (impurity) in profit arises from lack of ownership, exemplified by ghasp (usurpation). This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute: after a usurper compensates the owner and sells the property at a profit, must that profit be given in charity? Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say yes, while Abu Yusuf says it is halal. We will unpack their proofs and understand how the nature of the usurped property (determinable vs. non-determinable) affects the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State clearly the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (majority) and the ruling of Imam Abu Yusuf on profit after damān. Note which is considered mufta bihi.
The majority view is from Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Trace the core disagreement
Pinpoint the legal basis of the ikhtilaf: does payment of damān retroactively purify the usurped property, removing the khabth? Abu Yusuf says yes; the majority says no.
3
Analyze the majority's dalil for determinable property
For a usurped slave-girl (determinable), the contract attaches to her exact essence (haqiqah). The profit inherits the impurity because the sale occurred while she was not owned by the seller.
Real attachment means the contract cannot be fulfilled by a substitute.
4
Analyze the majority's dalil for non-determinable property
For usurped dirhams (non-determinable), the contract attaches by semblance (shabah) because the safety of the sold item depends on them. Even though dirhams are not individualised, their role in the transaction creates a link that transmits the impurity.
The text distinguishes two scenarios: explicit designation vs. mere estimation.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
The practical consequence: if a usurper compensates and sells at a profit, the majority obligates charity; Abu Yusuf permits keeping it. The mufta bihi is the majority view, which is more cautious regarding ill-gotten gains.
The text presents the majority's argument in greater detail, indicating tarjih.

لیکن اگر نفع میں عدم ملک کی وجہ سے فساد پیدا ہوا مثلاً ایک شخص نے باندی غصب کی اور مالک کو اس کی قیمت کا ضمان ادا کر کے اس کو فروخت کر کے نفع اٹھایا مثال در اہم غصب کئے اور ان کا نعمان ادا کیا پھر ان در اہم کے عوض کوئی چیز خرید کر اس کو فروخت کیا او نفع حاصل کیا تو طرفین کے نزدیک دونوں صورتوں میں نفع کا صدقہ کرنا واجب ہے کیونکہ جب عدم ملک کی وجہ سے خبث پیدا ہو تو وہ متعین اور مالا تعین دونوں مؤثر ہوتا ہے اور امام ابو یوسف نے فرمایا ہے کہ یہ نفع غاصب کے لئے حلال ہے۔ امام ابو یوسف کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ غاصب نے جب مئی منصوبہ کا ضمان ادا کر دیا تو وہ اس کا مالک ہو گیا اور اس نے نفع اپنی مملوکہ چیز سے اٹھایا ہے نہ کہ غیر مملوکہ سے اور مملوکہ چیز سے نفع اٹھانا جائز ہے اور لئے یہ نفع غاصب کے لئے حلال ہو گا اس کا صدقہ کرنا واجب نہ ہوگا۔ طرفین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ عقد، مایتعین کے ساتھ حقیقی متعلق ہوتا ہے یعنی جب باندی غصب کر کے اس کو فروخت کیا تو یہ عقد بیع اسی مغصو بہ باندی کے ساتھ متعلق ہوگا کیونکہ بائع کے لئے اس باندی کی جگہ دوسری باندمی کا سپر د کرنا جائز نہیں ہے اور جب عقد بیع حقیقت منصو بہ باندی کے ساتھ متعلق ہے تو اس سے جو نفع حاصل ہوگا اس میں بھی حقیقت خبث متحقق ہوگا اور چونکہ حقیقت خبث حلال نہیں ہے اس لئے اس نفع کا صدقہ کرنا واجب ہوگا اور مالا تعیین کے ساتھ عقد بیع کا تعلق شبہہ ہوتا ہے۔ یعنی جب درا ہم غصب کر کے ان کے عوض کوئی چیز خریدی تو یہ خریدنا ان دراہم کے ساتھ شہری متعلق ہوگا کیونکہ جب دراہم مغصوبہ کے عوض کوئی چیز خریدی تو اس کی دو صورتیں ہیں تو ان در اہم مغصوبہ کی طرف اشارہ کیا۔ مثلا یوں کہا کہ ان دراہم کے عوض یہ چیز خریدی اور انھیں میں سے ادا کیا یا ان دراہم مخصو بہ کی طرف اشارہ تو کیا مگر شمن ان کے علاوہ میں سے ادا کیا پس پہلی صورت میں دراہم منصوبہ کے ساتھ سلامت مبیع متعلق ہے کیونکہ جب در اهم مغصوبہ ہی شمن واقع ہیں تو انھیں کی وجہ سے غاصب کے لئے مبیع سلامت رہے گی۔ اور جب دراہم منصو بہ ،سلامت طبع کا سبب بنے تو ان کے ساتھ اگر چہ حقیقتہ عقد بیع متعلق نہیں ہوگا کیونکہ در اہم ودنا نیر متعین کرنے سے متعین نہیں ہوتے لیکن چونکہ وہ سلامت طبیع کا تہ ہیں اس لئے ان کے ساتھ عقد کا تعلق شبت ضرور ہو گا اور دوسری صورت میں دراہم منصوبہ کے ساتھ تقدیر یمن متعلق ہے یعنی در اہم
But if the impurity arises due to lack of ownership, for example, a person usurped a slave-girl and, after paying the owner the guarantee of her value, sold her and made a profit; or example, he usurped dirhams and paid their guarantee, then bought something with those dirhams and sold it and made a profit, then according to both parties (the side of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, and the side of Abu Yusuf) in both cases it is obligatory to give the profit in charity, because when impurity arises due to lack of ownership, it is effective on both determinable and non-determinable. But Imam Abu Yusuf said that this profit is halal for the usurper. Imam Abu Yusuf's evidence is that when the usurper paid the guarantee of the usurped property, he became its owner, and he made profit from his owned property, not from unowned property, and making profit from owned property is permissible. Therefore, this profit is halal for the usurper; it is not obligatory to give it in charity. The evidence of the other two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that the contract attaches to the determinable item in reality. That is, when he usurped a slave-girl and sold her, this contract of sale attaches to that same usurped slave-girl, because the seller cannot deliver another slave-girl in her place. And when the contract of sale attaches to the usurped slave-girl in reality, then the profit obtained from it will also have real impurity, and since real impurity is not halal, it is obligatory to give that profit in charity. And the attachment of the contract of sale with non-determinable items is by semblance. That is, when a person usurped dirhams and bought something with them, this purchase attaches to those dirhams by semblance, because when he bought something in exchange for the usurped dirhams, there are two scenarios: either he pointed to those usurped dirhams, e.g., said 'I bought this thing in exchange for these dirhams' and paid from them, or he pointed to the usurped dirhams but paid from other than them. In the first scenario, the safety of the sold item is attached to the usurped dirhams, because when the usurped dirhams themselves are the price, the sold item remains safe for the usurper due to them. And when the usurped dirhams become the cause of safety of the sold item, then the contract attaches to them by semblance, even though it does not attach in reality, because dirhams and dinars are not determinable by specification. But since they are the cause of safety, the contract's attachment to them by semblance is necessary. In the second scenario, the estimation of the price is attached to the usurped dirhams, i.e., the dirhams.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of khabth (impurity) in profit, now focusing on the second type: khabth due to lack of ownership ('adam al-milk), exemplified by ghasp (usurpation). The author presents a case: a usurper takes a slave-girl (determinable) or dirhams (non-determinable), then compensates the owner by paying the guarantee (damān), then sells the property and makes a profit. The two sides (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad vs. Abu Yusuf) differ on whether that profit must be given in charity. The general rule is that when impurity arises from lack of ownership (as in usurpation until ownership is restored by payment of damān), it affects both determinable and non-determinable items. So both parties initially agree that charity is obligatory. However, Imam Abu Yusuf argues that once the usurper pays the guarantee, he becomes the owner of the usurped property retroactively; thus, the profit is from his own property and is halal. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad counter that even after payment of damān, the original usurped property (the slave-girl) still carries the impurity because the sale contract attached to its exact essence; the profit inherits that impurity. For dirhams, they acknowledge that the attachment is by semblance (shabah) only, because dirhams are non-determinable, but due to the usurpation, the profit still requires charity. The passage then delves into two scenarios regarding usurped dirhams: one where the usurper specifically designates the usurped dirhams as the price, and one where he does not, and discusses how the attachment by semblance works in each. The overall lesson is that the distinction between the two types of khabth (due to fasid milk vs. due to lack of milk) is critical: the former only affects determinable items, while the latter affects both. The intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf shows the depth of reasoning in the madhhab.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasp): Unlawful taking of another's property without permission; usurpation. The property remains owned by the original owner until the usurper pays damān (guarantee), which then transfers ownership.
ضمان (damān): Guarantee or compensation payment made by the usurper to the owner for the usurped property; it transfers ownership of the property to the usurper.
شبہ (shabah): Semblance or analogous attachment; used here to describe how a contract attaches to non-determinable items (like dirhams) due to their role in the transaction, even though the contract does not attach to their essence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, if a person usurps a slave-girl, pays the owner her value (damān), then sells her and makes a profit, what is the ruling on that profit?
A) The profit is halal because the usurper now owns the slave-girl
B) The profit must be given in charity because the contract attached to the usurped slave-girl in reality
C) The profit must be returned to the original owner
D) The profit is permissible only if the usurper did not intend to profit

Key Takeaway
Profit from usurped property must be given in charity even after compensation, because the contract's attachment (real or semblance) perpetuates the impurity of usurpation.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

10 min
Profit from Defective Ownership: Determinate vs Indeterminate Assets
Distinguishes between determinable and non-determinable assets regarding tainted profit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mā yataʿayyan and mā lā yataʿayyan regarding profit from defective ownership, and apply the ruling to both fasad al-milk and ʿadam al-milk cases.

Core Idea
Profit from a determinate asset with defective ownership must be given in charity; profit from indeterminate assets is halal.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a critical nuance in Hanafi contract law: how to treat profit when ownership is defective. The key is whether the asset is determinate (mā yataʿayyan) or indeterminate (mā lā yataʿayyan). The ruling also depends on whether the defect is in the ownership itself (fasad al-milk) or the total absence of ownership (ʿadam al-milk). We will walk through the distinctions and the evidence provided in the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two asset categories
Determine whether the asset is mā yataʿayyan (a specific, non-fungible item like a slave or land) or mā lā yataʿayyan (fungible item like dirhams/dinars). This classification determines the nature of the contract's relation to the asset.
Hanafis treat mā yataʿayyan as the actual object of the contract; mā lā yataʿayyan is only related by shubha (suspicion).
2
Classify the ownership defect
Two types: fasad al-milk (defective ownership, e.g., from a voidable sale) and ʿadam al-milk (total absence of ownership, e.g., usurpation/ghasb). The impurity (khubth) grading differs.
Fasad al-milk: profit from one's own property, so taint is doubtful. ʿAdam al-milk: profit from another's property, taint is real.
3
Apply the fasad al-milk rule
For fasad al-milk, real taint (khubth) becomes shubhat al-khubth (doubtful impurity) for determinate assets, and further weakens to shubha shubhat al-khubth for indeterminate assets. Only the former is actionable, so charity is wajib for determinate, halal for indeterminate.
The hadith 'نهى عن الربا أو الريبة' (prohibition of ribā and doubt) supports that even doubtful impurity must be avoided.
4
Apply the ʿadam al-milk (ghasb) rule
In ghasb, taint is real for both categories according to the Sahibayn (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). Prophet's ḥadīth indicates even doubtful impurity (shubha) prohibits. Abu Yusuf dissents once compensation is paid.
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf: Abu Yusuf permits keeping profit; Sahibayn require charity.
5
Synthesize the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of the Sahibayn for ghasb (charity wajib for both categories). For fasad al-milk, the distinction stands: charity only for determinate assets.
Authority weight: Zahir al-Riwayah; tarjih by Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah.

شہرتہ الخبث تو معتبر ہے لیکن اس سے نیچے کا شبہ یعنی شیبہ شہبتہ النبث معتبر نہیں ہے اس لئے فساد ملک کی صورت میں مایتعین کے اندر جو شبہتہ النحبت پیدا ہوا ہے اس کا صدقہ کرنا واجب ہے لیکن مالا یعین کے اندر جو شبہہ شبتہ النحبث پیدا ہوا ہے اس کا صدقہ کرنا واجب نہیں ہے۔
The author of Hidayah says: The difference between mā yataʿayyan (determinate) and mā lā yataʿayyan (indeterminate) lies in the impurity whose cause is fasad al-milk (defective ownership). That is, if impurity occurs in mā yataʿayyan due to fasad al-milk, then it is not halal for him, rather it is wajib to give it in charity. And if impurity occurs in mā lā yataʿayyan due to fasad al-milk, then it is halal, it is not wajib to give it in charity, as the detail has passed previously. But if the impurity in the profit is due to ʿadam al-milk (absence of ownership), for example, a person usurped a slave-girl and paid compensation to the owner, then sold her and made a profit; similarly, he usurped dirhams, paid compensation for them, then bought something with those dirhams and sold it and made a profit – then according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), in both cases it is wajib to give the profit in charity, because when impurity arises due to absence of ownership, it is effective in both determinate and indeterminate. And Imam Abu Yusuf said that this profit is halal for the usurper. Imam Abu Yusuf's evidence is that when the usurper paid compensation for the mā yataʿayyan, he became its owner, and he made profit from his owned property, not from unowned property, and making profit from owned property is permissible. Therefore this profit is halal for the usurper, giving it in charity is not wajib. The evidence of the two is that a contract is actually related to mā yataʿayyan. That is, when he usurped a slave-girl and sold her, this sale contract pertains to that usurped slave-girl, because it is not permissible for the seller to deliver another slave-girl in her place. And when the sale contract pertains to the usurped slave-girl in reality, then the impurity in the profit realized from it is also real. And since real impurity is not halal, giving that profit in charity is wajib. And for mā lā yataʿayyan, the relation of the sale contract is shubha (suspicion). That is, when he usurped dirhams and bought something with them, this purchase relates to those dirhams by shubha, because when he buys something with usurped dirhams, there are two scenarios: either he pointed to those usurped dirhams, e.g., said 'I bought this thing for these dirhams' and paid from them, or he pointed to those usurped dirhams but paid from others. In the first scenario, safety of the purchased item pertains to the usurped dirhams, because when the usurped dirhams are the actual price, the purchased item is safe for the usurper due to them. And when the usurped dirhams are the cause of safety, then even though the sale contract does not actually relate to them (because dirhams and dinars are not determined by specification), nevertheless, since they are the basis of safety, the contract relates to them by shubha necessarily. In the second scenario, the relation to the usurped dirhams is by way of estimation (taqdīran), i.e., even though the usurped dirhams were not paid, the price was estimated with reference to them, because it is necessary to mention the type, quantity, and quality of the price, and all these are known through the usurped dirhams. So the sale contract does not actually relate to the usurped dirhams, but there is shubha. The conclusion is that the relation of the sale contract to usurped dirhams is by shubha. And when the relation is by shubha, the impurity in the profit is also by shubha. And since both real impurity and shubha impurity are not halal, giving both in charity is wajib. The evidence for both not being halal is this hadith: 'The Prophet ﷺ prohibited ribā (usury) and ar-ribā (doubtful matters), meaning he prohibited both ribā and shubha ribā.' And in the case of fasad al-milk, since the profit is not obtained from another's property but from one's own property (though with defective ownership), in the case of fasad al-milk, real impurity turns into shubha al-khubth (doubtful impurity), and shubha al-khubth turns into shubha shubhat al-khubth (doubt of doubtful impurity). The result is that in the case of fasad al-milk, in mā yataʿayyan, shubhat al-khubth arises, and in mā lā yataʿayyan, shubha shubhat al-khubth arises. In sharia, shubhat al-khubth is considered, but the level below that, i.e., shubha shubhat al-khubth, is not considered. Therefore, in the case of fasad al-milk, for mā yataʿayyan, the shubhat al-khubth that arises makes it wajib to give in charity, but for mā lā yataʿayyan, the shubha shubhat al-khubth that arises does not make it wajib to give in charity.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah clarifies the ruling regarding profit earned from an asset that was acquired through a defective contract (fasad al-milk). The key distinction is between assets that are determinate (mā yataʿayyan), like specific slaves or land, and those that are indeterminate (mā lā yataʿayyan), like dirhams and dinars. When the ownership defect is due to fasad al-milk (e.g., a sale that is later deemed invalid but the buyer had possession), the profit from a determinate asset is considered impure (khubth) and must be given in charity (ṣadaqah). However, the profit from an indeterminate asset is halal and no charity is required. This is because the defect in ownership is only a legal defect, not a real taint from another's property; the profit is from one's own property. The impurity becomes merely doubtful (shubha), and for indeterminate assets, the doubt is even weaker (shubha of shubha) and therefore not actionable. The passage then contrasts this with cases where the profit arises from true absence of ownership (ʿadam al-milk), such as usurpation (ghaṣb). Here, both Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the profit must be given in charity regardless of whether the asset is determinate or indeterminate, because the taint is real. Imam Abu Yusuf dissents, arguing that once the usurper compensates the owner, he becomes the owner and the profit is halal. The passage details the reasoning of each side, emphasizing that for determinate assets, the contract actually pertains to the specific item, while for indeterminate assets, the relation is only by way of suspicion (shubha). A hadith prohibiting ribā and shubha ribā is cited to support the view that both real and doubtful impurity must be avoided. Finally, the passage concludes by applying the same principle to fasad al-milk: only the stronger doubt (shubhat al-khubth) is considered, not the weaker one (shubha shubhat al-khubth).

Key Terms
مایتعین (mā yataʿayyan): An asset that is determined by specification, e.g., a specific slave or piece of land; incapable of substitution in a contract.
مالا تعین (mā lā yataʿayyan): An asset that is not determined by specification, such as dirhams or dinars; they are fungible and can be substituted.
خبث (khubth): Impurity or taint in wealth that renders it unlawful (ḥarām) for the owner, necessitating charity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of fasad al-milk (defective ownership) for mā lā yataʿayyan (indeterminate assets), what is the ruling on the profit?
A) It is wajib to give it in charity.
B) It is halal and no charity is required.
C) It is haram and must be returned to the seller.
D) It is makruh but permissible.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between profit from determinable and indeterminable assets when ownership is defective, with wajib charity only for determinable assets in fasad al-milk and for all assets in ghasb (Sahibayn view).

Profit from Usurped Property After Compensation: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on profit after ghaṣb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on profit from usurped property after compensation, explain the evidence for each view, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Profit from usurped property after compensation is wajib to give in charity (Sahibayn), not halal (Abu Yusuf).

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding profit obtained from usurped (ghaṣb) property after the usurper has compensated the original owner. The difference in rulings depends on whether compensation transfers full ownership, as argued by Imam Abu Yusuf, or whether the taint of absence of ownership remains, as argued by the Sahibayn. Understanding this distinction clarifies how the Hanafi school resolves conflicts between ownership principles and transactional integrity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Recognize that the passage deals with two scenarios: a determinate (muʿayyan) asset like a slave-girl, and an indeterminate (ghayr muʿayyan) asset like dirhams. Both scenarios raise the same question: after the usurper pays compensation, does the profit become lawful?
2
State the Sahibayn’s position and evidence
The Sahibayn (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold that the profit must be given in charity. Their evidence: for determinate assets, the sale contract relates to the specific usurped item, so the profit carries the same impurity (khabāth). For indeterminate assets, the relation is by shubha (suspicion), but the impurity remains. Hence charity is wajib.
3
State Abu Yusuf’s position and evidence
Abu Yusuf holds that the profit is halal. His evidence: once the usurper pays the value, he becomes the owner of the item; the profit is then from his own property, and profit from owned property is permissible.
4
Determine the mufta bihi position
According to Zahir al-Riwayah and the later Hanafi authorities (e.g., al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah), the position of the Sahibayn is the relied-upon one. Therefore, the profit must be given in charity.

But if the corruption in the profit arises due to absence of ownership, for example, a person usurped a slave-girl and paid compensation to the owner, then sold her and made a profit; similarly, he usurped dirhams, paid compensation for them, then bought something with those dirhams and sold it and made a profit – then according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), in both cases it is wajib to give the profit in charity, because when impurity arises due to absence of ownership, it is effective in both determinate and indeterminate. And Imam Abu Yusuf said that this profit is halal for the usurper. Imam Abu Yusuf's evidence is that when the usurper paid compensation for the determinate property, he became its owner, and he made profit from his owned property, not from unowned property, and making profit from owned property is permissible. Therefore this profit is halal for the usurper, giving it in charity is not wajib. The evidence of the two is that a contract is actually related to determinate property. That is, when he usurped a slave-girl and sold her, this sale contract pertains to that usurped slave-girl, because it is not permissible for the seller to deliver another slave-girl in her place. And when the sale contract actually pertains to the usurped slave-girl, then the impurity in the profit realized from it is also actual. And since actual impurity is not halal, giving that profit in charity is wajib. And for indeterminate property, the relation of the sale contract is by way of suspicion (shubha).
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding profit obtained from usurped (ghaṣb) property after the usurper has compensated the original owner. The two main positions are: (1) The Sahibayn (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that the profit must be given in charity, whether the asset is determinate (like a slave-girl) or indeterminate (like dirhams). Their reasoning is that the sale contract actually relates to the specific usurped item for determinate assets, so the profit is tainted with real impurity. For indeterminate assets, the relation is by shubha, but still the profit is impure. (2) Imam Abu Yusuf holds that once the usurper pays the value, he becomes the genuine owner, and the profit from his own property is halal. The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that of the Sahibayn, as established in Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
عدم ملک (ʿadam al-milk): Absence of ownership; the state where the seller never had ownership of the item, e.g., in usurpation.
ضمان (ḍamān): Compensation paid to the original owner for the usurped property, making the usurper owner in the view of Abu Yusuf.
خبث (khabāth): Impurity or taint that renders profit impermissible to keep; must be purified by charity.
متعین / مالا تعیین (muʿayyan / mā lā yataʿayyan): Determinate (specific, identifiable) vs. indeterminate (fungible, replaceable) property.
شبہ (shubha): Suspicion or ambiguity; a weaker form of legal relation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school, what must be done with profit from a usurped item after the usurper has compensated the owner?
A) The profit is halal and may be kept.
B) The profit must be given in charity.
C) The profit must be returned to the buyer.
D) The profit is makruh but permissible.

Key Takeaway
Profit from usurped property after compensation is wajib to give in charity according to the relied-upon Hanafi position, even though Imam Abu Yusuf held it halal.

Profit from Money Received on a False Claim is Halal
Ruling on profit from a false debt claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between fasad al-milk and ʿadam al-milk to determine the permissibility of profit from money received under a false claim.

Core Idea
Profit from money received on a false claim is halal because the defect is only in ownership (fasad al-milk), not in the money itself.

The case seems paradoxical: a plaintiff falsely claims a debt, receives payment, then admits the debt was invalid, yet the profit from that money is halal. The Hanafi school provides a precise legal reasoning based on the nature of ownership and the fungibility of money. This lesson unpacks that reasoning, focusing on the concepts of fasad al-milk (defective ownership) and mā lā yataʿayyan (indeterminate assets).

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the question
Understand the facts: a plaintiff claims a debt, defendant pays, then both admit no debt was owed. The plaintiff made a profit with the money. The question: is the profit halal or must it be given in charity?
The key is to distinguish this from a case where the money itself is haram (e.g., stolen).
2
Differentiate fasad al-milk from ʿadam al-milk
Fasad al-milk (defective ownership) means the possessor has some right to the asset, but the acquisition is legally flawed. ʿAdam al-milk means no ownership at all. The impurity (khubth) is weaker in fasad al-milk.
Hanafi usul: for fasad al-milk, the impurity in indeterminate assets (mā lā yataʿayyan) is considered shubha, not decisive.
3
Trace the reasoning in the text
The debt became due by the claim (even if false), then invalidated by mutual admission. The money paid is a substitute (badal) for the invalid debt, so it is owned by the plaintiff. Since the money is indeterminate (dirhams), the impurity does not affect it.
The principle: for fungibles, the defect in ownership does not make the profit haram.
4
Derive the ruling
The profit is halal (mubah) and no charity (sadaqah) is required. This is the mufta bihi Hanafi position.
This is based on talil reasoning from the text, not explicit dalil naqli.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وكذا اذا ادعى على اخر ما لا فقضاه اياه ثم تصادقا انه لم يكن عليه شئى وقد ربح المدعي في الدراهم يطيب لـه الـربـح لان الخبث لفساد الملك ههنا لان الدين وجب بالتسمية ثم استحق بالتصادق وبدل المستحق مملوك فلا يعمل فيما لايتعين
ترجمہ اور اسی طرح اگر ایک نے دوسرے پر مال کا دعوی کیا پس مدعی علیہ نے مدعی کو ادا کر دیا پھر دونوں نے باہم سچائی کے ساتھ کہا کہ مدعی علیہ پر کچھ مال نہ تھا حالانکہ مدعی نے ان دراہم میں نفع اٹھایا ہے تو مدعی کے لئے نفع حلال ہے کیونکہ یہاں خبث فساد ملک کی وجہ سے ہے کیونکہ قرضہ مدعی کے دعوی کرنے سے واجب ہوا ہے پھر باہمی تصدیق سے یہ قرضہ مستحق ہو گیا ہے اور اس قرضہ مستقہ کا بدل مملوک ہے اس لئے خبث ان دراہم میں مؤثر نہ ہو گا جو متعین نہیں ہوتے ہیں۔ تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ایک آدمی نے دوسرے پر دعویٰ کیا کہ میرے تجھ پر ایک ہزار روپیہ ہیں لہذا تم ایک ہزار رو پیادا کرو ۔ پس مدعی علیہ نے ایک ہزار روپیہ مدعی کو ادا کر دیئے پھر دونوں اس پر متفق ہو گئے کہ مدعی مدعی علیہ پر کچھ واجب نہیں ہے حالانکہ اس عرصہ میں مدعی نے مدعی علیہ کی اس رقم سے نفع حاصل کر لیا تو یہ نفع مدعی علیہ کے لئے حلال ہے اس کا صدقہ کرنا واجب نہیں ہے کیونکہ یہاں خبث فساد ملک کی وجہ سے ہے اور فساد ملک کی وجہ سے خبث اس لئے ہے کہ مدعی کے ایک ہزار روپیہ کا دعوی کرتے وقت مدعی علیہ کے اقرار کی وجہ سے مدعی علیہ پر ایک ہزار روپیہ کا دین ( قرضہ ) واجب ہوا ہے اور دین ما وجب فی الزمہ کو کہتے ہیں پس جب مدعی علیہ نے مدعی کو ایک
He said: Similarly, if someone claims against another a debt and the defendant pays it to him, then both admit that nothing was owed to him, and the plaintiff has made a profit with those dirhams – the profit is halal for him, because the impurity here is due to fasad al-milk (defective ownership). This is because the debt became due by virtue of the claim, then it is proven to have been invalid by their mutual admission, and the substitute of what is proven invalid is owned (by the plaintiff), so the impurity does not affect what is not determinate (dirhams).
Translation: And similarly, if a person claimed property from another, and the defendant paid it, then both truthfully said that nothing was owed to him, while the plaintiff made a profit with those dirhams – the profit is halal for the plaintiff, because here the impurity is due to fasad al-milk. This is because the debt became due by the plaintiff's claim, then by mutual admission this debt became invalid, and the substitute of that invalid debt is owned. Therefore, the impurity does not affect those dirhams, which are not determinate.
Explanation: The scenario is that a person claimed from another: 'I have a thousand rupees on you; pay me a thousand rupees.' Then the defendant paid a thousand rupees to the plaintiff. Then both agreed that nothing was owed to the plaintiff on the defendant, while in the meantime the plaintiff made a profit from that amount. This profit is halal for the plaintiff; it is not wajib to give it in charity, because here the impurity is due to fasad al-milk (defective ownership). The impurity due to fasad al-milk is because at the time the plaintiff claimed a thousand rupees, due to the defendant's admission (or perhaps the claim itself), a debt (dīn) of a thousand rupees became due on the defendant. And 'dīn' is what is due in the liability (dhimmah). Then when the defendant paid the plaintiff, and later they mutually admitted that there was no debt, the money became owned by the plaintiff (as a substitute for the invalid debt). Since the defect is only in the ownership (fasad al-milk), not in the money itself, and the money is mā lā yataʿayyan (indeterminate), the impurity does not affect it, so the profit is halal.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific scenario: a plaintiff (madʿī) falsely claims a debt from a defendant (madʿī ʿalayhi). The defendant pays, and both later admit the debt was not owed. Meanwhile, the plaintiff made a profit using that money. The ruling is that the profit is halal for the plaintiff and does not need to be given in charity. The reasoning is that the impurity (khubth) in this case is due to fasad al-milk (defective ownership), not due to ʿadam al-milk (absence of ownership). The money was paid based on a claim that created a debt in the dhimma, but that debt was later invalidated. The money itself is mā lā yataʿayyan (indeterminate), and as established in the previous lesson, for fasad al-milk, the impurity in indeterminate assets is only shubha shubhat al-khubth, which is not considered in sharia. Hence the profit is halal. The commentary also defines dīn as 'what is due in the liability' (mā wujiba fī al-dhimmah).

Key Terms
دین (dīn): A debt that is due in the liability (dhimmah) of a person, here arising from a claim and later invalidated.
فساد ملک (fasad al-milk): Defective ownership; the possessor has some right but the ownership is legally flawed, e.g., from a voidable contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the reason the profit is halal in the case described?
A) Because the plaintiff owned the money outright.
B) Because the impurity is due to fasad al-milk and the money is indeterminate.
C) Because the defendant admitted the debt initially.
D) Because the profit was small.

Key Takeaway
Profit from money received under a false claim is halal in the Hanafi school because the defect is only in ownership, not in the money itself, and the money is fungible.

Shubha and Khabuth: Charity on Profit from Usurped Dirhams
Explaining the effect of shubha on profit from usurped goods and the ruling on charity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between shubhat al-khabuth and weaker shubha (shubhat shubhat al-khabuth) in transactions involving defective ownership (fasad al-milk), and determine when it is wajib to give the profit in charity.

Core Idea
The strength of shubha determines whether charity is wajib on profit from defective ownership.

When a transaction involves usurped goods as the price, the profit may be tainted. The Hanafi school distinguishes between two levels of shubha (doubt) in defective ownership: a stronger one (shubhat al-khabuth) that requires purification through charity, and a weaker one that does not. This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah explains this key distinction using a hadith as proof.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
The transaction involves usurped dirhams used as the price (thaman). The contract's connection to these dirhams is not real but based on shubha (doubt).
Usurped goods are not owned by the seller, so the contract is defective (fasad al-milk).
2
Identify the ruling on profit
Profit from a transaction with shubha carries shubha of impurity (khabuth). Such profit is not halal, and it must be given in charity.
The hadith forbidding riba and shubha of riba is cited as proof.
3
Analyze the dalil
The hadith 'He forbade riba and shubha of riba' indicates that both actual riba and doubtful transactions are prohibited. Therefore, profit from a doubtful transaction is not permissible.
The hadith is ahad, so its thubut and dalala are zanni.
4
Distinguish between fasad al-milk cases
In defective ownership, if the property is determinate (muta'ayyin), the shubha is shubhat al-khabuth (strong). If indeterminate (ghayr muta'ayyin), the shubha is weaker (shubhat shubhat al-khabuth).
This distinction is crucial in Hanafi fiqh.
5
Apply the distinction to charity obligation
Charity is wajib only for profit arising from determinate property (shubhat al-khabuth). For indeterminate property, the weaker shubha is not considered, so charity is not wajib.
The mufta bihi position follows this differentiation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيوع

اشرف البدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد هشتم

مغصوبہ اگر چہ ادا نہیں کئے مگر ان کے ساتھ ثمن کا اندازہ کیا گیا ہے کیونکہ ثمن کی جنس اس کی مقدار اور وصف کا بیان کرنا ضرورت ہے اور یہ سب باتیں دراہم مغصوبہ کے ذریعہ معلوم ہوئیں ہیں پس دراہم مغصوبہ کے ساتھ عقد بیع کا تعلق حقیقتہ تو نہ ہوگا لیکن شبہ یہ ضرور ہوگا حاصل یہ کہ دراہم مغصوبہ کے ساتھ عقد بیع کا تعلق شبہیہ ہے۔ اور جب عقد بیع کا تعلق دراہم مغصوبہ کے ساتھ شبہتہ ہے تو اس عقد سے جو نفع ہو گا اس میں خبث بھی شبہ ہوگا۔ اور چونکہ حقیقت حلت اور شبہہ خبث دونوں حلال نہیں ہیں اس لئے ان دونوں کا صدقہ کرنا واجب ہے اور ان دونوں کے حلال نہ ہونے کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے ان النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم نهى عن الربا او الريبة یعنی نبی اکرم صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے ربا اور شبہ ربا دونوں سے منع فرمایا ہے اور فساد ملک کی صورت میں نفع چونکہ غیر کے مال سے حاصل نہیں ہوتا بلکہ اپنے مال سے حاصل ہوتا ہے اگر چہ اس میں ملک فاسد ہے اس لئے فساد ملک کی صورت میں حقیقت خبث بدل کر شبہ حلت ہو جائے گا۔ اور شبہتہ الخبث بدل کر شبهتہ النخبث ہو جائے گا۔ حاصل یہ کہ فساد ملک کی صورت میں متعین کے اندر شبہتہ الخبث پیدا ہوگا۔ اور غیر متعین کے اندر شبہ شبهتہ الخبث پیدا ہوگا اور شریعت میں شبہتہ الخبث تو معتبر ہے لیکن اس سے نیچے کا شبہ یعنی شبہ شبهتہ الخبث معتبر نہیں ہے اس لئے فساد ملک کی صورت میں متعین کے اندر جو شبہتہ الخبث پیدا ہوا ہے اس کا صدقہ کرنا واجب ہے لیکن غیر متعین کے اندر جو شبہہ شبهتہ الخبث پیدا ہوا ہے اس کا صدقہ کرنا واجب نہیں ہے۔
Book of Sales. If usurped dirhams are not paid, but the price (thaman) is estimated through them, because it is necessary to describe the type, quantity, and quality of the price, and all these things are known through the usurped dirhams. So the connection of the contract of sale with the usurped dirhams is not real but it is a doubt (shubha). When the contract is connected with usurped dirhams through shubha, then the profit from this contract will also have shubha of impurity (khabuth). Since both real lawfulness (halal) and shubha of impurity are not halal, it is obligatory to give both in charity. The proof for their not being halal is this hadith: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade riba and shubha of riba.' In the case of defective ownership (fasad al-milk), profit is not acquired from someone else's property but from one's own property, even though ownership is defective. Therefore, in the case of fasad al-milk, real impurity becomes shubha of lawfulness, and shubha of impurity becomes shubha of shubha of impurity. The conclusion: In fasad al-milk, in determinate property (muta'ayyin), shubha of impurity arises; in indeterminate property (ghayr muta'ayyin), shubha of shubha of impurity arises. In the Shari'ah, shubha of impurity is considered, but the lower shubha, i.e., shubha of shubha of impurity, is not considered. Hence, in fasad al-milk, the shubha of impurity that arises in determinate property, it is obligatory to give it in charity; but the shubha of shubha of impurity that arises in indeterminate property, it is not obligatory to give it in charity.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the ruling on profit from a transaction where usurped goods are used as the price. The author first clarifies that when the price is estimated using usurped dirhams, the connection is not real but based on a doubt (shubha). Consequently, the profit generated carries a shubha of impurity. Based on a hadith forbidding both riba and shubha of riba, such profit is not halal, and charity is wajib. The key distinction is made between two types of defective ownership (fasad al-milk): In determinate property (muta'ayyin), the shubha is stronger (shubhat al-khabuth), and charity is wajib. In indeterminate property (ghayr muta'ayyin), the shubha is weaker (shubhat shubhat al-khabuth), and charity is not wajib. This distinction is crucial in Hanafi fiqh for determining when tainted profit must be purified.

Key Terms
شبہہ (shubha): A doubt or ambiguity in a transaction that affects its legal purity.
خبث (khabuth): Impurity or unlawfulness in property or profit due to prohibited means.
متعین (muta'ayyin): Specific, determinate property that is identified.
فساد ملک (fasād al-milk): Defective ownership where the title is legally flawed but not void.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a transaction with usurped goods (fasad al-milk), which type of property requires charity on the profit?
A) Determinate property (muta'ayyin)
B) Indeterminate property (ghayr muta'ayyin)
C) Both types
D) Neither type

Key Takeaway
The stronger shubha (shubhat al-khabuth) in determinate defective property requires charity; the weaker shubha in indeterminate property does not.
